Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
8 min
The Maxim: No Ijtihad in the Face of a Text
Explaining the seventh major maxim with types of ijtihad and the condition for prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'No ijtihad where there is a text' by distinguishing between texts that bar ijtihad and those that allow it.

Core Idea
A definitive text bars ijtihad; ambiguous texts invite it.

This lesson unpacks one of the five major maxims from the Majalla: 'La masagha lil-ijtihad fi mawrid al-nass' (Article 14). This principle governs the relationship between textual evidence and juristic reasoning. A mufti must know when a text is so clear that it leaves no room for interpretation or analogy. Misapplying this maxim can lead to invalid fatwas that either disregard clear revelation or improperly restrict legitimate ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Begin with the Arabic maxim and its English meaning. Note that it is one of the major qawaid kubra and applies across all fiqh chapters, especially in understanding texts and performing qiyas.
2
Define key terms: nass and ijtihad
Identify what constitutes a 'text' (nass) in this context—Quranic verses, authentic hadith, and reliably transmitted consensus. Then define ijtihad as the exertion of scholarly effort to derive rulings, distinguishing two types: understanding the text and analogy.
In Hanafi usul, nass is a category of clear text that does not admit ta'wil when it is qat'i.
3
Identify when ijtihad in understanding is prohibited
Ijtihad in understanding the text is necessary when the text is ambiguous. However, if the text is explicit and clear, no ijtihad may contradict its apparent meaning. Use the example of the hadith on burden of proof to illustrate a clear text that prohibits reversal.
The hadith 'Al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i' is so clear that a judge cannot allocate proof and oath differently.
4
Identify when ijtihad by analogy is prohibited
Analogy cannot be used when a text exists for the ruling. If a hadith is authentic (all transmitters reliable), it is followed and analogy is abandoned. If authenticity is disputed, those who deem it authentic follow it; others may resort to analogy.
This shows that a text must be established (thabit) to bar analogy; disputed texts leave room for ijtihad.
5
Summarize the condition for prohibition
The prohibited ijtihad is only that which contradicts a text that is definitive in both transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalalah). If either is uncertain, ijtihad is permissible.
This condition is called 'qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah' in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السابعة : لا مسَاغَ للاجتهاد في مورد النص» (م/ ١٤).
النص: خطاب الشارع، وهو آيات القرآن والأحاديث النبوية الصحيحة، أي الثابتة .
ولكن المراد بالنص هنا ما يشمل الإجماع الثابت بالنقل الصحيح. والاجتهاد بذل الجهد العلمي في استنباط الأحكام الشرعية من أدلتها، وهو نوعان:
- اجتهاد الفقهاء في فهم النص التشريعي.
- اجتهادهم في قياس حكم لا نصَّ فيه على حكم منصوص عليه.
أ - فأما الاجتهاد في فهم النص فلا بد منه مع وجود النص لمعرفة تطبيقه . ولكن محله إنما هو عند غموض النص واحتماله وجوهاً مختلفة تفسيره وتطبيقه .
فإذا كان النص صريحاً واضحاً في إفادة الحكم الذي ورَدَ لأجله بصورة لا تحتمل التأويل لم يجز تأويله بما يُخرجه عن ظاهره، أو لا يحتمله . فمثلاً قول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام: البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» صريح واضح في قبول البينة من المدعي لأجل الإثبات فلا يسوغ لأحد أن يجتهد في فهمه بعكس هذا التوزيع، فيقبل البينة من المدعى عليه ولا يقبلها من المدعي أو يجعل الخيار للحاكم في تكليف من يشاؤه من الخصمين المتقاضيين بالبينة أو اليمين، ومنع الآخر منها .. أما قبول بيّنةٍ معاكسة من المُدَّعى عليه للترجيح بينها وبين بينة المدعي فإنه لا ينافي هذا النص، لأنَّ هذا التدبير لا يمنع المدعي من الإثبات الذي منحه إياه النص، وإنما هو وسيلة لتمحيص البينات والقضاء بالأقوى.
ب - وأما الاجتهاد عن طريق القياس، فلا يجوز الالتجاء إليه مع وجود نص ثابت في الحكم المطلوب معرفته لأنَّ محل القياس إنما هو عند عدم النص. على أنه قد يوجد حديث نبوي مروي في موضوع فيراه بعض الفقهاء صحيحاً (أي رواته كلهم ثقات يعتمد على روايتهم) فيأخذ به ويترك القياس المخالف له بينما لا يراه غيره بالغاً درجة الصحة، فيتركه ويلجأ إلى القياس .
يستخلص من ذلك أنَّ الاجتهاد الممنوع في مورد النص هو ما كان مصادماً لنص ثابت وواضح في المعنى الذي ورد فيه وضوحاً لا يحتمل التأويل (١).
(۱) وهو ما يسمى في علم أصول الفقه : قطعي الثبوت والدلالة.
The Seventh Maxim: 'There is no scope for ijtihad where there is a text' (Majalla article 14). 'Text' (nass) means the address of the Lawgiver, which is the verses of the Quran and the authentic prophetic hadiths, i.e., those that are established. But what is meant by text here includes consensus established by authentic transmission. Ijtihad is the exertion of scholarly effort in deriving legal rulings from their evidences, and it is of two types: (1) The ijtihad of jurists in understanding the legislative text. (2) Their ijtihad in analogizing a ruling for which there is no text to a textually ruled case. A. As for ijtihad in understanding the text, it is indispensable with the existence of the text to know its application. But its scope is only when the text is ambiguous, admitting various possible interpretations and applications. If the text is explicit and clear in conveying the ruling for which it came, in a manner that does not admit interpretation, it is not permissible to interpret it in a way that removes it from its apparent meaning, or that it does not accommodate. For example, the Prophet's saying (peace be upon him): 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on him who denies' is explicit and clear in accepting the proof from the claimant for the purpose of proof. So it is not permissible for anyone to exercise ijtihad in understanding it in a way contrary to this distribution, such as accepting the proof from the defendant and not from the claimant, or giving the judge the option to charge whichever of the litigants he wishes with proof or oath and prevent the other. However, accepting a counter-proof from the defendant for weighing between it and the claimant's proof does not contradict this text, because this measure does not prevent the claimant from proving what the text granted him; rather, it is a means to scrutinize the proofs and adjudicate with the stronger. B. As for ijtihad by way of analogy, it is not permissible to resort to it when there is a fixed text concerning the desired ruling, because the place of analogy is only when there is no text. However, it may happen that a prophetic hadith is narrated on a subject, and some jurists deem it authentic (i.e., all its transmitters are reliable and their narration is relied upon), so they adopt it and leave the contrary analogy, while others do not deem it to have reached the degree of authenticity, so they leave it and resort to analogy. From this it is deduced that the ijtihad that is prohibited in the presence of a text is that which contradicts a fixed text that is clear in meaning, clarity that does not admit interpretation (1). (1) And this is what is called in usul al-fiqh: qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah (definitive in transmission and indication).
Source pages p. 950

Commentary
This passage from al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Aam explains the seventh maxim of the Majalla: 'There is no scope for ijtihad where there is a text.' The author first defines key terms: 'nass' refers to divine address (Quran and authentic hadith) and also includes consensus that is reliably transmitted. 'Ijtihad' is the scholarly effort to derive rulings from evidence. He distinguishes two types of ijtihad: (1) ijtihad in understanding the text, and (2) ijtihad by analogy (qiyas). For the first type, ijtihad is necessary when the text is ambiguous and admits multiple interpretations. But if the text is explicit and unequivocal, no ijtihad is allowed that distorts its apparent meaning. He gives the example of the hadith 'Al-bayyinah 'ala al-mudda'i...' which clearly places the burden of proof on the claimant and the oath on the defendant. Any ijtihad that reverses this allocation is prohibited. However, accepting a counter-evidence from the defendant for evaluation does not contradict the text, as it aids in determining the stronger proof. For the second type, analogy cannot be used when there is a text on the matter. However, if a hadith's authenticity is disputed, those who consider it authentic follow it and abandon analogy, while those who do not consider it authentic resort to analogy. In conclusion, prohibited ijtihad is only that which contradicts a text that is definitive in both transmission and meaning (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah).

Key Terms
النص (al-nass): The address of the Lawgiver: Quranic verses and authentic prophetic hadiths, and according to the author, also consensus reliably transmitted.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The exertion of scholarly effort (bazl al-juhd al-'ilmi) in deriving legal rulings from their evidences. Two types: understanding the text and analogy.
قطعي الثبوت والدلالة (qat'i al-thubut wa al-dalalah): Definitive in both transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalalah); a text that is unquestionably from revelation and unequivocal in its import.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim, when is ijtihad prohibited in the presence of a text?
A) Whenever any relevant hadith exists, even if weak.
B) Only when the text is explicitly clear and definitive in transmission and meaning.
C) When the text is from the Quran only.
D) It depends on the mujtahid's preference.

Key Takeaway
The maxim prohibits ijtihad only when the text is definitive in both transmission and meaning; ambiguous texts require ijtihad for understanding and application.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Maxim 8: Ijtihad is Not Overturned by Its Like
Explaining the eighth major maxim with historical and modern applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, explain its justification with the example of 'Umar, and apply it to scenarios of changing ijtihad by the same or different mujtahids.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's ruling stands once issued; neither his own new ijtihad nor another's can overturn it retroactively.

This lesson introduces a foundational legal maxim that ensures stability and finality in ijtihad-based rulings. Drawing on historical precedent from Caliph 'Umar and modern court practice, the maxim clarifies that ijtihad does not retroactively void earlier decisions. Understanding this principle is essential for muftis and judges to maintain consistency and respect for past judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim's scope
The maxim applies to all ijtihad-based fatwas and judgments. It governs both the same mujtahid over time and different mujtahids simultaneously.
Scope is general across all fiqh chapters.
2
Understand the two scenarios
Scenario A: a mujtahid changes his own ijtihad; his prior ruling remains, new ijtihad applies only to new cases. Scenario B: two mujtahids differ; neither overturns the other's ruling.
Both illustrate the maxim's core: 'like ijtihad' cannot overturn an existing ijtihad.
3
Analyze the reasoning ('illa)
The reason is to prevent instability in legal rulings. If every change of ijtihad could undo settled cases, no ruling would ever be final.
This is rooted in the need for legal certainty and finality.
4
Examine the historical proof
Caliph 'Umar gave two different rulings on similar cases and was asked about it. He replied, 'That was according to what we ruled, and this is according to what we rule.' This shows that past rulings are not overturned.
This statement became a well-known precedent for the maxim.
5
Compare to modern legal principle
Higher courts that change their interpretation do not retroactively apply the change to already settled cases. This parallels the maxim in contemporary legal systems.
Thus the maxim is not confined to Islamic law but reflects a universal legal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
وهذا مبدأ مقرّر في النظر القانوني أيضاً فلا مجال للقضاة أن يجتهدوا في أحكامهم مع وجود نص قانوني صريح بخلاف اجتهادهم، لأن مهمة القاضي التطبيق لا التشريع.
٥٦/٨١ - القاعدة الثامنة : الاجتهاد لا ينقض بمثله» (م/١٦).
فمن اجتهد في حكم حادثة فأفتى بها أو قضى، ثم وقعت أخرى نظيرها فتبدل فيها اجتهاده إلى حكم مخالف لا تنقض فتواه أو قضاؤه السابقان وإنما يعمل باجتهاده الجديد في الحادثة الجديدة، إذ لو سَاغ نقض العمل بالفتاوى والأحكام الاجتهادية كلما تبدل اجتهاد المجتهد لما استقرَّ حكم في حادثة، لأنّ الاجتهاد عُرْضَةٌ للتبدل دائماً بتبدل وجهات النظر في الدليل.
وقد اشتهر عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه قضى في حادثة بحكم، ثم رفع إليه نظيرها فتبدل اجتهاده وقضى فيها بخلاف قضائه الأول، فقيل له في ذلك فأجاب: تلك على ما قضينا وهذه على ما نقضي». وقد جرت كلمته هذه مجرى الأمثال (ر) حاشية الحموي على الأشباه لابن نجيم تحت هذه القاعدة ١٤١/١).
وهذا مبدأ قانوني مقرَّر أيضاً ؛ فإن محاكم النقض العليا، التي يعود إليها النقض والإبرام إذا تبدل اجتهادها القضائي في حادثة أو في فهم مادة قانونية لا يسري ذلك على ما مضى؛ فلا ينقض ما قد بُت فيه من قضايا وإنما يعمل باجتهادها الجديد في القضايا الجديدة.
وكما لا ينقض اجتهاد المجتهد بتبدل اجتهاده نفسه لا ينقض باجتهاد مجتهد غيره، بل على كل مجتهد أن يحترم اجتهاد سواه، لعدم المرجح بعد تحقق رتبة الاجتهاد فيهما .
Chapter (81): Explanation and Classification of the Maxims of the Majalla
This is also an established principle in legal theory: judges are not allowed to exercise ijtihad in their rulings when an explicit legal text exists, contrary to their ijtihad, because the judge's task is application, not legislation.
56/81 - The eighth maxim: 'Ijtihad is not overturned by its like.' (Article 16)
So whoever exercises ijtihad in a ruling of a case and gives a fatwa or judgment, then another similar case occurs and his ijtihad changes to a contradictory ruling: his previous fatwa or judgment is not overturned; rather, he acts on his new ijtihad in the new case. For if overturning the operation of ijtihad-based fatwas and judgments were permissible whenever the ijtihad of a mujtahid changes, no ruling would ever be settled in a case, because ijtihad is always susceptible to change as perspectives on evidence change.
It is famous from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, that he gave a ruling in a case, then a similar case was brought to him and his ijtihad changed, and he ruled contrary to his first ruling. He was asked about that and replied: 'That was according to what we ruled, and this is according to what we rule.' This saying became proverbial. (See: al-Hamawi's footnote on al-Ashbah of Ibn Nujaym, under this maxim, 1/141)
This is also an established legal principle: The highest courts of cassation, which have the authority of reversal and confirmation, if their judicial ijtihad changes in a case or in understanding a legal provision, it does not apply retroactively; previous settled cases are not overturned; rather, they act on their new ijtihad in new cases.
And just as a mujtahid's ijtihad is not overturned by his own changed ijtihad, it is also not overturned by the ijtihad of another mujtahid. Rather, every mujtahid must respect the ijtihad of others, since there is no preponderant factor after the rank of ijtihad is established in both.
Source pages p. 952

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the eighth major maxim: 'Ijtihad is not overturned by its like.' The maxim means that when a mujtahid (a qualified legal scholar) issues a fatwa or judgment on a case based on his ijtihad, and later his ijtihad changes regarding a similar case, his previous fatwa or judgment remains valid and is not overturned. The new ijtihad applies only to future cases. The author provides two illustrations: first, the historical example of Caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, who gave two different rulings on similar cases without overturning the first; second, the modern analogy of supreme courts that do not retroactively apply changes in legal interpretation to already settled cases. Additionally, the maxim applies equally between different mujtahids: no mujtahid may overturn the ruling of another mujtahid of equal rank, because there is no basis to prefer one over the other once both have attained the level of ijtihad. This principle ensures stability and finality in legal rulings.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): The effort of a qualified scholar to derive a legal ruling from the sources
ينقض (yanquḍ): To overturn or annul a previous legal ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a judge's ijtihad changes on a case similar to one he previously ruled on?
A) He must overturn his previous ruling.
B) His previous ruling remains, and he applies the new ijtihad to the new case.
C) He must follow the opinion of another mujtahid.
D) He cannot change his ijtihad at all.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Ijtihad is not overturned by its like' ensures stability by preserving past rulings while allowing new ijtihad for future cases.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Maxim: What is Established Contrary to Analogy Cannot Be Analogized
Defining a maxim and illustrating it with furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ma thabata 'ala khilaf al-qiyas la yuqas 'alayhi' to identify when an exceptional ruling can or cannot be extended by analogy, and evaluate the furuat provided.

Core Idea
An exceptional ruling cannot be extended by analogy to other cases unless the same underlying cause applies.

This lesson unpacks the ninth maxim from the Majalla: a ruling that contradicts the normal analogical reasoning cannot be used as a precedent for further analogies. The maxim protects the integrity of general principles by confining exceptions to their specific legislative reason. We will explore the classic example of a will (wasiyya) and see how it applies to other contracts and to the institution of waqf.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its purpose
Begin by stating the maxim in Arabic and English: 'ما ثبت على خلاف القياس لا يقاس عليه' – 'What is established contrary to analogy cannot be analogized to others.' Understand that this maxim prevents the uncontrolled expansion of exceptional rulings.
The maxim is Article 15 of the Majalla.
2
Identify the exceptional ruling (wasiyya)
Recognize that a bequest (wasiyya) is an exception: it transfers ownership after death, when rights normally end. The Lawgiver allowed it for the special reason of enabling charity.
Wasiyya is limited to one-third of the estate unless heirs consent.
3
Apply the maxim to other contracts
Use the maxim to argue that other contracts (gift, loan, sale, lease) cannot be attached to after death by analogy. They lack the same legislative consideration (charity).
The exception does not become a general rule.
4
Recognize the nuance: analogizing by the same cause
Note the author's remark: if an exception is based on a specific 'illa (e.g., removing hardship), then the exception can be extended to other cases sharing that same 'illa. This does not violate the maxim.
Example: the permissibility of istihsan to remove hardship.
5
Apply the maxim to waqf rulings
Note that waqf rulings are derived by analogy from wasiyya, as the mutawalli's responsibility is analogous to an executor's. This is allowed because the 'illa of enabling charity is present in both.
Waqf is itself an exception but is derived via qiyas from another exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٧/٨١ - القاعدة التاسعة ما ثبَتَ على خلاف القياس لا يقاس عليه، أو ما ثبت على خلاف القياس فغيره عليه لا يقاس (م/ (١٥) .
رأينا في مناسبات عدة أن كثيراً من الأحكام تقرره الشريعة على خلاف القياس، أي تعطيه حكماً استثنائياً على خلاف مقتضى القواعد العامة السارية على أمثاله لوجود اعتبارات تشريعية خاصة به.
فالوصية مثلاً تمليك مضاف لحالة ما بعد الموت، وهي الحالة التي تنقطع فيها حقوق الشخص في أمواله وتتعلق بها حقوق ورثته. فالوصية ثابتة على خلاف القياس، لأنَّ الشارع أراد أن يفتح بها للشخص باباً يتدارك به ما فاته من أعمال البر والإحسان التي قد تشحُ بها نفسه حال الحياة خشية من الاحتياج. فلا يجوز أن يقاس عليها تجويز إضافة غيرها من التصرفات إلى ما بعد الموت كالهبة والإعارة، والبيع والإجارة مثلاً؛ بأن يعقدها الشخص في حياته عقداً مضافاً إلى ما بعد الموت
أما لو أوصى بأن يباع بعد موته شيء معين من تركته لفلان بنصف قيمته مثلاً، أو أن يعار له، فهذا سائغ لأنه وصية بالبيع أو الإعارة، لا عقد حاضر مضاف الأثر إلى ما بعد الموت.
ملاحظة :
ويجب أن يلحظ أن ما ثبت على خلاف القياس يمكن أن يقاس عليه في العلة التي خالف فيها القياس كدفع الحرج وسد الذرائع حيثما دعت الحاجة إليهما .
هذا وقد قالوا إن أحكام الوقف تستقى بطريق القياس من أحكام
الوصية، فتقاس مثلاً مسؤولية متولي الوقف على مسؤولية الأوصياء في تنفيذ
الوصية .
57/81 - The ninth maxim: 'What is established contrary to analogy cannot be analogized to others.' Or: 'What is established contrary to analogy, other things cannot be analogized to it.' (Article 15)
We have seen on several occasions that many rulings are established by the Shariah contrary to analogy, i.e., it gives an exceptional ruling contrary to the requirement of the general rules applicable to similar cases due to specific legislative considerations.
For example, a bequest (wasiyya) is a transfer of ownership attached to the state after death, a state where a person's rights in his property cease and the rights of his heirs attach. The wasiyya is established contrary to analogy, because the Lawgiver intended through it to open for a person a door to make up for acts of righteousness and charity that his soul may have been stingy with during life for fear of need. So it is not permissible to analogize to it permitting attaching other transactions to after death, such as gift (hiba), loan (i'ara), sale (bay'), and lease (ijara), by the person entering into a contract during his life attached to after death.
But if he bequeaths that a specific item from his estate be sold after his death to someone for half its value, for example, or that it be lent to him, this is permissible because it is a bequest of sale or loan, not a present contract whose effect is attached to after death.
Note:
It must be observed that what is established contrary to analogy can be analogized to regarding the cause ('illa) for which it contravened analogy, like removing hardship and blocking pretexts, wherever there is need for them.
Moreover, they (the jurists) said that the rulings of waqf (endowment) are derived by way of analogy from the rulings of wasiyya, so for example the responsibility of the mutawalli (waqf administrator) is analogized to the responsibility of executors in executing the wasiyya.
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage explains the ninth maxim: a ruling that is established as an exception to the general principle of qiyas (analogical reasoning) cannot itself be used as a basis for further analogy. The author gives the example of a will (wasiyya): normally, a transfer of ownership after death is invalid because a person's rights end at death, but the Shariah permits a will as an exception, allowing up to one-third of the estate to be given to non-heirs. This exceptional ruling cannot be extended to other contracts like a gift or sale that would take effect after death; those are not permitted because they lack the special legislative consideration (the need to allow charity after death). However, a bequest that instructs an executor to sell an item or lend it after death is valid because it is a form of wasiyya itself, not a separate contract. The author adds an important nuance: the exception itself can be used as a basis for analogy when the underlying cause ('illa) is the same, such as removing hardship or blocking evil outcomes. Furthermore, the rulings of waqf are derived by qiyas from those of wasiyya, as seen in the responsibility of the mutawalli being analogous to that of an executor.

Key Terms
خلاف القياس (khilaf al-qiyas): A ruling that departs from the normal analogical reasoning due to a special legislative reason.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest of up to one-third of the estate to non-heirs, valid after death.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause or rationale that justifies a legal ruling, including exceptions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the ninth maxim, why can a gift (hiba) taking effect after death not be analogized to a will (wasiyya)?
A) Because a gift is not a contract.
B) Because the exception of wasiyya is based on a special legislative reason (charity) that does not apply to gifts.
C) Because a gift requires immediate transfer and cannot be postponed.
D) Because a will is limited to one-third of the estate.

Key Takeaway
The ninth maxim: what is established contrary to analogy cannot be analogized to other cases, unless the same effective cause ('illa) applies.

The Tenth Maxim: When the Preventer Is Removed, the Prevented Returns
Maxim stated with cross-reference to earlier theory of contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع', identify its scope and exceptions, and apply it to construct a fatwa in cases of temporary legal obstacles.

Core Idea
A right or obligation suspended by a temporary obstacle is restored when the obstacle ends.

Legal systems distinguish between permanent prohibitions and temporary preventers. This maxim captures the Hanafi principle that when a temporary obstacle (man') that prevented a right or obligation from taking effect is removed, the original right or obligation revives. Understanding this maxim is crucial for the mufti to correctly rule on cases where an impediment has ceased, ensuring that rights are not permanently lost due to a transient condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maxim’s Formulation
The maxim 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع' consists of two parts: 'al-mani' (the preventer) and 'al-mamnu' (the prevented). Grasp that the maxim applies only when the preventer is a temporary legal obstacle, not an inherent absence of a condition or a permanent prohibition.
The Arabic term 'man'' implies an external, removable barrier; compare with 'adam' (absence) which is not covered.
2
Identify the Locus of Application
List the types of rights or obligations that can be 'prevented': e.g., inheritance rights, contractual obligations, duties of worship. Not all legal effects are subject to this maxim; only those that are originally established but suspended.
In contract law, a sale may be suspended due to a defect; the right to rescind returns when the defect is cured.
3
Distinguish Permanent vs. Temporary Preventers
A preventer is temporary if it can be removed (e.g., illness, travel, missing condition). Prohibitions by sacred law (e.g., marrying a mahram) are not removed; thus the maxim does not apply.
A common error is to apply the maxim to cases where the original act is outright forbidden; that is a different category.
4
Apply to Concrete Cases (Furuat)
Given a scenario, determine whether a preventer exists, whether it is temporary, and whether its removal restores the original right or obligation. Use examples like inheritance after murder, Hajj after illness, or a contract after a third-party objection is lifted.
For each case, verify that the prevented right was present before the preventer; the maxim does not create new rights.
5
Construct a Fatwa Using the Maxim
When issuing a fatwa, state the factual obstacle, confirm its removal, and then apply the maxim to reinstate the original ruling. Include any exceptions (istithna'at) that limit the maxim’s scope.
Always check that no other maxim or textual proof overrides this one in the specific case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٨/٨١ - القاعدة العاشرة : إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع (م (۲٤) .
تقدم أول نظرية العقود في البحث عن المانع شرح هذه القاعدة (ر:
ف ۷/۲۸).
58/81 - The tenth maxim: 'When the preventer is removed, the prevented returns.' (Article 24)
The explanation of this maxim has preceded in the first part of the theory of contracts in the discussion of the preventer (see: volume 7, p. 28).
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This passage introduces the tenth maxim of the Majalla: 'When the preventer (man') is removed, the prevented (mamnu') returns.' The maxim means that if a legal right or obligation is temporarily suspended due to an obstacle, it reverts to its original state once the obstacle is lifted. For example, the right to inherit for an heir who was a murderer is prevented, but if the murder was unintentional or the heir repents, the right may return. The author does not elaborate further here but refers the reader to an earlier section. In Hanafi fiqh, this maxim applies in various contexts such as contracts, inheritance, and worship. For instance, if someone is prevented from performing Hajj due to illness, the obligation returns when health is restored.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani'): A legal preventer that temporarily suspends a right or obligation
الممنوع (al-mamnu'): The right or obligation that is prevented from taking effect
القاعدة العاشرة (al-qa'idah al-'ashirah): The tenth maxim of the Majalla: 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A person who intentionally kills his father is later pardoned by the court; he now inherits.
B) A woman who was too poor to perform Hajj later becomes wealthy; she must now perform Hajj.
C) A man divorces his wife irrevocably; later he reconciles; the marriage is reinstated.
D) A contract is void due to lack of consent; after consent is obtained, the contract becomes valid.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'When the preventer is removed, the prevented returns' applies only to temporary obstacles that suspend an already existing right or obligation; permanent prohibitions and cases where the right never existed are excluded.

The Tenth Maxim: Removal of Obstacle Restores the Blocked Ruling
States the tenth major maxim and references its prior explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع', identify its rank among the major maxims, and apply it to a simple Hanafi furu' case.

Core Idea
When the legal obstacle (mani') vanishes, the blocked rule springs back into effect.

This lesson introduces one of the classical Hanafi legal maxims: 'If the obstacle is removed, the prevented thing returns.' The maxim governs how legal impediments affect the application of rulings. Although the passage is brief, it points to an earlier explanation in the text. Understanding this maxim is essential for any mufti who must determine when a previously blocked transaction or ritual becomes valid again.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Commit the Arabic text to memory: إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع. This is a foundational maxim.
2
Understand the concepts
Identify the three key terms: zawaal (removal), maani' (obstacle), and mamnuu' (prevented thing). The mamnuu' is the legal effect that was blocked.
3
Distinguish mani' from shart and sabab
A mani' is a bar that, if present, prevents a ruling from taking effect. Unlike a shart (condition) which must be present for validity, or a sabab (cause) which triggers a ruling, the mani' blocks an otherwise valid ruling. When the mani' is removed, the ruling returns as if the mani' never existed.
4
Apply a simple furu'
Example: A person is declared legally incompetent (safih) and is prevented from managing his wealth. When he regains competency, his right to transact returns. This illustrates the maxim: the obstacle (incompetence) is removed, so the prevented thing (capacity to contract) returns.
Ensure the example is Hanafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٥٨/٨١ - القاعدة العاشرة : إذا زال المانع عاد الممنوع (م (٢٤) ) . تقدم أول نظرية العقود في البحث عن المانع شرح هذه القاعدة (ر: ف 7/28).
58/81 - The Tenth Maxim: If the obstacle is removed, the prevented thing returns (Majalla article 24). The explanation of this maxim has preceded in the first theory of contracts in the discussion of the obstacle (see: p. 7/28).
Source pages p. 953

Commentary
This is a brief statement of one of the major legal maxims of the Hanafi school, also found in the Majalla. The maxim means that when a prohibition or bar (mani') that prevented a legal effect from occurring is lifted, the original ruling that was blocked is reinstated. For example, if a person is prevented from performing a contract due to lack of capacity, once capacity is regained, the contract can be made. The author refers to an earlier explanation in the book for details.

Key Terms
المانع (al-mani'): A legal obstacle that prevents a ruling from taking effect.
الممنوع (al-mamnū'): The prevented thing; the legal effect or ruling that is blocked by the obstacle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the obstacle is removed, the prevented thing returns.
B) If the cause is present, the effect follows.
C) Remove hardship.
D) Custom is authoritative.

Key Takeaway
When a legal obstacle (mani') is removed, the ruling that was blocked is reinstated, but not retroactively.

The Maxim: Temporary Excuses Have Expiry
Explaining a maxim with examples from devotional and judicial matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله' to novel cases and identify its scope.

Core Idea
Temporary excuses lift prohibitions only for their duration; when the excuse ends, the original ruling returns.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi legal maxim that governs how concessions based on temporary excuses expire. The maxim is succinct: 'What is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ceases.' We will explore its meaning through examples from worship (fasting, duress) and judicial matters (deposits during a fire). Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti to ensure that dispensations do not persist beyond their necessity.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Write the maxim in Arabic and its English meaning. Understand that it is a recognized qawida sughra (minor principle) in Hanafi fiqh.
The maxim is codified in the Majalla (article 23).
2
Identify the two categories of examples
The passage gives examples in 'matters of religious devotion' and 'judicial matters'. Identify the common thread: a temporary excuse temporarily changes the ruling.
The excuse must be temporary; permanent excuses (like being a traveler) are governed by other principles.
3
Trace the reversal in each example
For each example, list the ruling before the excuse, during the excuse, and after the excuse ceases. Note how the original ruling restores.
The maxim focuses on the moment the excuse ends.
4
Determine the scope
The maxim applies across all fiqh chapters but especially in ibadat and muamalat. It does not apply to permanent concessions or to acts already validly performed.
Example: Tayammum for a prayer is valid even if water is found after prayer; the prayer is not invalidated.
5
Identify limitations and related maxims
Note that there are no stated exceptions (istithna'at) in this passage. The maxim complements 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed) but is descriptive, not prescriptive.
A mufti must ensure the excuse is genuinely temporary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله» (م۲۳) .
- ففي أمور الديانة : إذا زال المرض المسوّغ للإفطار خلال رمضان وجب الصيام فيما بقي.
وإذا زال الاضطرار المبيح للمحظور ارتفعت الإباحة. وهكذا . . . - وفي الأمور القضائية : إذا شب حريق في دار؛ فأخرج صاحبها ما عنده من ودائع وسلمها لبعض الجيران فهو معذور في ذلك، ولا يضمنها إن تلفت، فإذا انتهى الحريق فتركها عندهم فهلكت كان مقصراً، فيضمنها لزوال عذره الذي سَوّغ وضعها لديهم.
What is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ceases. (Majalla art. 23). - In matters of religious devotion: if the illness that allowed breaking the fast during Ramadan ends, fasting becomes obligatory for the remaining days. And if the duress that made prohibited things permissible ends, the permissibility is removed. And so on. - In judicial matters: if a fire breaks out in a house, and the owner takes out the deposits he has and hands them over to some neighbors, he is excused in that, and he is not liable if they are damaged. But when the fire ends and he leaves them with them and they are damaged, he is negligent and liable because his excuse that allowed placing them with them has ceased.
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This passage presents the legal maxim: what is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ends. The author provides two categories of examples. First, in matters of worship (أمور الديانة): if a person is ill and breaks his fast during Ramadan, once he recovers, he must fast the remaining days. Similarly, if one is under duress (اضطرار) and thus allowed to do something normally prohibited, when the duress ends, the prohibition returns. Second, in judicial matters (الأمور القضائية): if a fire threatens deposits, the owner may temporarily leave them with a neighbor; he is not liable for loss during the fire. But after the fire, if he neglects to retrieve them and they are lost, he is liable because the excuse has ended. This maxim is a key principle ensuring that temporary dispensations do not persist beyond their necessity.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): An excuse that legally permits an otherwise prohibited act or omission.
اضطرار (idtirar): A state of necessity that makes prohibited things permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A Muslim may eat pork if starving; once full, he may continue eating pork.
B) A sick person breaks his fast; after recovery, he must fast remaining days.
C) A traveler may shorten prayer; after arriving home, he continues shortening.
D) A debtor may delay payment if bankrupt; when wealthy, he may still delay.

Key Takeaway
The maxim ensures that legal concessions due to temporary excuses do not outlast their necessity.

Maxim: What is Forbidden to Take is Forbidden to Give
Explaining a maxim with examples and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'ما حرم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه' to identify forbidden transactions and distinguish exceptions based on necessity.

Core Idea
Giving a forbidden thing makes you a partner in sin; but necessity can shift the prohibition to the taker.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi qawida that extends the prohibition of taking unlawful items to giving them. The rationale is that giving facilitates and encourages the sinful act. However, the maxim has exceptions where genuine necessity or coercion may excuse the giver, placing the sin solely on the taker. Understanding this maxim helps a mufti navigate muamalat cases involving prohibited earnings.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rationale
Identify the maxim and the reasoning: giving assists and encourages the forbidden act, making the giver a partner in sin.
The maxim is from the Majalla (art. 35).
2
Apply the maxim to standard cases
Consider examples: bribery, usury, wages for wailing. Confirm that both giving and taking are forbidden.
These are clear cases of haram earnings.
3
Identify the exceptions
Three exceptions are given: payments to evil-doers without authority, paying usury under duress, and guardian paying a usurper to rescue orphan's property. Note the governing qawida: 'الضرورة تبيح المحظورات' (Necessity permits the prohibited).
The common factor is genuine necessity or coercion.
4
Apply the exception criteria to new scenarios
Given a modern scenario (e.g., paying a bribe to avoid harm when no legal recourse exists), determine if the exception applies. The giver must be under genuine duress, not merely convenience.
The exception is narrow; most bribes are not excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما حَرُم أخذه حرم إعطاؤه» (م/
وذلك لأنَّ الإعطاء عندئذ إعانة وتشجيع على أخذ المحرم، فيكون المعطي شريك الآخذ في الإثم.
وعلى هذا لا يجوز إعطاء الرّشوة والربا، والأجرة على النياحة، وعلى سائر الأعمال الفسقية، كما لا يجوز أخذ ذلك .
الاستثناءات :
ويستثنى من هذه القاعدة ما يُدفع للأشرار والهجائين للتخلص من أذاهم ولسانهم عند فقدان السلطان الوازع وما يدفعه المستقرض بالربا في حالة الاضطرار إلى الاستقراض، وما يدفعه الوصي من بعض مال اليتيم إلى غاصبه لتخليص الباقي إذا لم يمكن تخليصه دون ذلك .
ففي جميع ذلك وأمثاله تكون الحرمة فيه على الآخذ دون المعطي.
What is forbidden to take is forbidden to give. (Majalla art. 35). Because giving in such a case is assistance and encouragement for taking the forbidden, so the giver becomes a partner in sin with the taker. Accordingly, it is not permissible to give bribery, usury, wages for wailing, and all other sinful acts, just as it is not permissible to take them. Exceptions: Excluded from this rule are what is given to evil-doers and satirists to get rid of their harm and tongue in the absence of an authority to restrain; what a borrower pays as usury under duress of necessity; and what a guardian pays from the orphan's property to a usurper to rescue the rest if it cannot be rescued otherwise. In all these and similar cases, the prohibition is on the taker, not the giver.
Source pages p. 954

Commentary
This passage introduces the maxim that if something is prohibited to take, it is also prohibited to give. The reasoning is that giving facilitates and encourages the prohibited act. For example, bribery (الرشوة), usury (الربا), and wages for forbidden acts like wailing (النياحة) are unlawful both to give and receive. The author then lists important exceptions: payments made to evil individuals to prevent harm when no authority exists; usury paid under necessity; and payment to a usurper to rescue an orphan's property. In these cases, the sin falls only on the taker, as the giver is compelled. This reflects the fiqh principle of balancing prohibitions with necessity.

Key Terms
رشوة (rishwah): Bribery: giving money to influence a decision unjustly.
ربا (riba): Usury or interest; forbidden in all transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Giving a bribe to secure a business contract.
B) Paying usury under duress of necessity.
C) Paying wages for lawful work.
D) Giving a gift to a judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim extends the prohibition of taking to giving, but necessity can excuse the giver while the taker remains sinful.

Maxim: Punishment by Deprivation for Hastening
Explaining a maxim with three furuat from faraid and family law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'whoever hastens something before its time is punished by deprivation' to at least three cases from inheritance, divorce, and marriage, and identify its policy basis in siyasah shar'iyyah.

Core Idea
The maxim penalizes abuse of rights by turning the intended premature gain into a loss.

This lesson introduces a powerful legal maxim that operates as a deterrent against abuse of rights. It is not a fixed penalty but a policy tool rooted in siyasah shar'iyyah and sadd al-dhara'i'. Through concrete examples from inheritance, divorce, and marriage, you will see how the law deprives a person of what they sought prematurely, transforming their intended gain into deprivation.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its legal basis
Read the Arabic maxim and note that it falls under siyasah shar'iyyah (Islamic policy) and sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil). Understand that the punishment (deprivation) is not punitive in a criminal sense but a deterrent to prevent circumvention of rights.
The maxim is recorded in the Majalla (art. 99) and is considered a sughra (minor) maxim.
2
Identify the common structure in the examples
Each example involves a person who attempts to obtain a legal benefit (inheritance, bequest, inheritance from wife, or marriage) before its proper time through a prohibited or harmful act. The maxim then turns the intended benefit into a loss.
The key is that the act is done with intent to unlawfully speed up the benefit.
3
Apply to the first example: killing the testator
A person kills the testator to inherit early. The ruling: the killer is deprived of inheritance and bequest. The proper time for inheritance is after death, and the murder hastens that time wrongfully.
This applies in both faraid and wasaya.
4
Apply to the second example: talaq al-firar
A husband divorces his wife during his terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance. If he dies during her iddah, he is deprived of inheriting from her. His intent to harm backfires.
This is called 'divorce of escape' (talaq al-firar) in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply to the third example: remarriage before iddah
A woman divorced by her husband remarries another before her iddah ends. Based on a fatwa of Umar ibn al-Khattab, if the second marriage is consummated, she becomes permanently prohibited to the new husband. She hastened the marriage, so she is deprived of that marriage.
This is based on maslahah mursalah and sadd al-dhara'i', not explicit text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه» (م/ ۹۹) .
هذه القاعدة من باب السياسة الشرعية في القمع وسد الذرائع. - مثال ذلك أن الإرث والوصية وقتهما الشرعي بعد موت المورث والموصي . فلو قتل شخص مورثه أو الموصي له بلا مسوّغ شرعي يحر بذلك من الإرث أو الوصية. - وكذلك لو طلق الرجل زوجته طلاقاً بائناً في مرض موته ومات خلال عدتها ترتُ منه منه في الاجتهاد الحنفي لدلالة مرض الموت على قصده حرمانها من الإرث، فيرد قصده عليه. وهذا يسمى : طلاق الفرار لأنَّ قصد فاعله التهرب به من إرث امرأته .
وهذا الحكم هو من صور منع التعسف في استعمال الحق في الفقه الإسلامي ر : ف ٧/٥ الحاشية و ٢٧/٥).
وقد نقل عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه أفتى في المرأة التي يطلقها زوجها فتتزوج غيره قبل انقضاء عدتها بأنها تحرم على هذا الزوج الثاني إن دخل بها حرمة مؤبدة معاملة لها بنقيض مقصودها بمقتضى السياسة الشرعية في المصالح المرسلة (ر ): تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي، من وضع كلية الشريعة في الأزهر ص/ ٤٧).
Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by deprivation (Majalla art. 99). This maxim falls under the category of Islamic policy (al-siyasa al-shar'iyya) in suppression and blocking means (sadd al-dhara'i'). - Example: inheritance and bequest have their legal time after the death of the testator and legator. So if a person kills his testator or legator without legal justification, he is deprived of the inheritance or bequest. - Similarly, if a man divorces his wife irrevocably during his terminal illness and dies during her waiting period, he is deprived of inheriting from her in the Hanafi juristic opinion, because the terminal illness indicates his intent to deprive her of inheritance; so his intent backfires. This is called 'divorce of escape' (talaq al-firar). This ruling is an instance of preventing abuse of rights in Islamic jurisprudence. It is reported that Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) gave a fatwa that if a woman is divorced by her husband and then marries another before completing her waiting period, she becomes permanently forbidden to the second husband if he consummates, as a treatment against her intention (mu'amalah bi naqid maqsudiha) based on maslahah mursalah.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage discusses the maxim that one who tries to obtain something before its legally prescribed time loses that thing. It is applied as a deterrent against abuse. Three examples are given: a killer of the testator loses inheritance/bequest; a husband who divorces his wife in terminal illness to deprive her of inheritance is himself deprived of inheriting from her (called talaq al-firar); and a woman who remarries during her iddah is permanently forbidden to the new husband if he consummates, based on a ruling of Umar. This reflects the principle of misuse of rights and the policy of sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means to evil).

Key Terms
طلاق الفرار (talaq al-firar): A divorce pronounced by a husband during terminal illness to deprive his wife of inheritance; in Hanafi fiqh, such divorce results in the husband being deprived of inheriting from her if he dies during her iddah.
سياسة شرعية (siyasah shar'iyyah): Governmental policy based on Shar'iah to achieve public interest; this maxim is an example.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Killing the testator
B) Talaq al-firar
C) Remarrying before iddah ends
D) Divorcing a wife to marry another

Key Takeaway
The maxim is a tool of siyasah shar'iyyah to prevent abuse of rights by turning premature gains into losses.

Maxim 12: Hastening Before Time — Penalty of Deprivation
Explanation of a legal maxim with three furu' applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Whoever hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation' to new cases and identify its role in Hanafi siyasah shar'iyyah.

Core Idea
If you rush to secure a benefit before its proper time, you lose it.

This lesson introduces a key legal maxim from the Majalla that serves as a deterrent against fraudulent timing. The rule is rooted in the principle of blocking means (sadd al-dhara'i') and is applied in inheritance, divorce, and marriage to prevent abuse of rights. Understanding this maxim helps you identify when an action intended to secure a benefit will instead nullify it.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Write the Arabic text and its meaning. Note that it is Article 99 of the Majalla.
The exact wording is: من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه
2
Identify the legal basis
The rule is part of siyasah shar'iyyah and sadd al-dhara'i' (blocking means). It is a policy to deter fraud.
This is not a purely textual derived rule but a policy maxim.
3
Analyze the first furu' (killing to inherit)
A person who kills the testator or legator to hasten inheritance is deprived of that benefit.
The killer's act is premature; the proper time for inheritance is after natural death.
4
Analyze the second furu' (divorce in terminal illness)
A man who divorces his wife irrevocably during terminal illness and dies in her iddah is considered to have intended to deprive her of inheritance. The Hanafi ruling: the wife inherits despite the valid divorce.
This is called talaq al-firar (divorce of escape). The husband's fraudulent intent is turned back against him.
5
Analyze the third furu' (remarriage during iddah)
Umar's fatwa: a woman who marries another before her iddah ends becomes permanently haram to the second husband if consummated.
This is a deterrent (siyasah) to prevent women from circumventing the iddah to quickly remarry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية عشرة: من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه» (م/ ۹۹) .
هذه القاعدة من باب السياسة الشرعية في القمع وسد الذرائع. - مثال ذلك أن الإرث والوصية وقتهما الشرعي بعد موت المورث والموصي . فلو قتل شخص مورثه أو الموصي له بلا مسوّغ شرعي يحر بذلك من الإرث أو الوصية. - وكذلك لو طلق الرجل زوجته طلاقاً بائناً في مرض موته ومات خلال عدتها ترتُ منه منه في الاجتهاد الحنفي لدلالة مرض الموت على قصده حرمانها من الإرث، فيرد قصده عليه. وهذا يسمى : طلاق الفرار لأنَّ قصد فاعله التهرب به من إرث امرأته .
وهذا الحكم هو من صور منع التعسف في استعمال الحق في الفقه الإسلامي ر : ف ٧/٥ الحاشية و ٢٧/٥).
وقد نقل عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه أفتى في المرأة التي يطلقها زوجها فتتزوج غيره قبل انقضاء عدتها بأنها تحرم على هذا الزوج الثاني إن دخل بها حرمة مؤبدة معاملة لها بنقيض مقصودها بمقتضى السياسة الشرعية في المصالح المرسلة (ر ): تاريخ الفقه الإسلامي، من وضع كلية الشريعة في الأزهر ص/ ٤٧).
The twelfth rule: Whoever hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation (Article 99). This rule is from the chapter of Shariah policy in suppression and blocking means. - Example of that is that inheritance and bequest, their legitimate time is after the death of the testator and the legator. So if a person kills his testator or the legator without legitimate excuse, he is thereby deprived of inheritance or bequest. - Likewise, if a man divorces his wife an irrevocable divorce during his terminal illness and dies within her waiting period, he is deprived of the [right to] inheritance in Hanafi ijtihad because the terminal illness indicates his intent to deprive her of inheritance, so his intent is turned back against him. This is called 'divorce of escape' because the doer's intent is to evade his wife's inheritance. This ruling is one of the forms of preventing abuse of rights in Islamic jurisprudence. And it is narrated from Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) that he gave a fatwa concerning a woman whose husband divorces her and she marries another before the expiry of her waiting period, that she becomes permanently prohibited to this second husband if he consummates the marriage, as a treatment contrary to her intended purpose, under the Shariah policy of discretionary public interest.
Source pages p. 955

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth legal maxim from the Majalla, a core qawida in Hanafi jurisprudence. The author classifies this rule as 'Shariah policy in suppression and blocking means'—it is a deterrent principle aimed at preventing individuals from manipulating timing to gain an unfair advantage. The three examples illustrate the maxim: killing a testator or legator results in loss of inheritance/bequest; a man who divorces his wife in terminal illness to deny her inheritance is himself deprived of the right to alter the inheritance; a woman who remarries before her iddah ends becomes permanently haram to the new husband. The last example is attributed to Umar's fatwa and is based on maslahah mursalah. Note that the Hanafi position on the divorce-in-terminal-illness case is based on the presumption of intent to defraud, following the principle that ends are judged by intentions.

Key Terms
طلاق الفرار (talaq al-firar): A divorce pronounced by a husband during terminal illness with the intent to prevent his wife from inheriting from him.
السياسة الشرعية (al-siyasah al-shar'iyyah): Shariah-based policy that governs rulings to achieve public interest and prevent harm, often used by rulers or judges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'Whoever hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation', what is the ruling for a man who divorces his wife in his terminal illness and dies during her iddah?
A) The wife loses her right to inheritance because the divorce is valid.
B) The husband is deprived of his right to alter inheritance; the wife inherits despite the divorce.
C) The divorce is invalid and the wife remains married.
D) The wife inherits but the husband's family may contest.

Key Takeaway
The maxim penalizes premature action to secure a benefit; it is a tool of Shariah policy to prevent fraud and abuse of rights.

Continuation Is Easier Than Commencement (Al-Baqa' Ashal Min Al-Ibtida')
Explanation of a legal maxim with derivative rule and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-baqa' ashal min al-ibtida'' to distinguish between tolerated subsequent defects and invalidating initial defects in contracts, and state the operative fatwa.

Core Idea
A contract defect arising after formation is tolerated; the same defect at inception invalidates it.

This lesson presents one of the fundamental Hanafi legal maxims: 'Continuation is easier than commencement.' This maxim has profound implications for contract law, allowing flexibility in ongoing contracts while maintaining strictness at formation. We will explore its meaning, its derivative rule, and key applications in ijarah, hibah, and sale.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its derivative
Read the two maxims: base rule 14 and its derivative, sub-rule 65. Understand that continuation is easier, so what is not tolerated at the outset may be tolerated during continuance.
The derivative rule is a dabt sub-maxim.
2
Identify the rationale
Recognize that the principle mirrors 'prevention is easier than removal.' The law facilitates ongoing contracts to avoid hardship.
3
Analyze the ijarah example
In a lease, the leased item must be distinct at inception; initial shuyu' invalidates. But if shuyu' arises later (e.g., inheritance), it is tolerated.
Contrast initial vs. subsequent shuyu'.
4
Analyze the hibah example
Similar to ijarah: a gift of divisible property requires clear identification at formation; later-acquired shuyu' does not invalidate.
5
Analyze the sale example
Buying one item by share from a lump sum is invalid because the price is unknown. But if all items were purchased and some reclaimed, the remaining sale valid with price adjustment.
This shows the maxim preserving the contract despite partial loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه

القاعدة الرابعة عشرة: البقاء أسهل من الابتداء) (م/(٥٦) .
كما أن الدفع أسهل من الرفع.
وعلى هذا يمكن أن يغتفر اختلال بعض شرائط العقود في حال استمرارها بعد وجودها، ولا يُغتفر ذلك في حال انعقادها.
ويتجلى ذلك في أمثلة القاعدة التالية المتفرعة عنها :
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد
٦٥/٨١ - يغتفر في البقاء مالا يغتفر في الابتداء» (م/ ٥٥) .
وذلك لأن البقاء ،أسهل، كما أفادته القاعدة السابقة :
- ومن أمثلتها ما تقدم من أن عقد الإجارة يشترط لصحته أن يكون المأجور متميزاً لا حصة شائعة وكذلك الهِبَة فيما يحتمل القسمة .
ولكن الفقهاء اغتفروا الشيوع الطارىء فيهما دون المقارن: (ر )) ف ٩/٢٥ - ١٠)، وقواعد الحمزاوي ص/ (٢٠٥ لأن الطارىء يكون في حالة بقاء العقد دون ابتدائه. . وكذلك لو كان المجموعة أشياء ثمن مجمل، فاشترى شخص أحدها بحصته من ثمنها لم يصح لجهالة ثمنه عند العقد، وهذا يسمى: البيع بالحصة .
ولكن لو اشتراها كلها ثم استحق من يده بعضها، فله أن يرجع على البائع بثمن ما استحق ويبقى البيع في الباقي بحصته من الثمن.
Chapter Ten: Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh

Rule Fourteen: Continuation is easier than commencement (art. 56).
Just as prevention is easier than removal.
Based on this, some conditions being defective may be tolerated during the continuance of contracts after their existence, but that is not tolerated at the time of their formation.
This is manifested in the examples of the following rule derived from it:
Derived rules:
65/81 – 'What is not tolerated at the outset is tolerated during continuance' (art. 55).
That is because continuation is easier, as the previous rule indicated.
- Among its examples is what was mentioned earlier: that for the validity of an ijarah contract, the leased item must be distinct, not a vague share, and likewise for hibah (gift) in what is divisible. However, the jurists tolerated subsequent undivided shares (shuyu') in them but not initial undivided shares, because subsequent shuyu' occurs in the state of the contract's continuance, not at its commencement. Also, if there is a group of items sold for a lump sum price, and a person buys one of them with its share of the price, the sale is invalid due to ignorance of the price at the time of the contract. This is called 'sale by share' (bay' bi al-hissah). But if he bought all of them and then some of them were rightfully claimed from his possession, he may recourse against the seller for the price of what was claimed, and the sale remains valid for the remaining items with their share of the price.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage presents two related legal maxims. The first, Base Rule 14, states that 'continuation is easier than commencement' (البقاء أسهل من الابتداء). The second, derived from it, is Sub-Rule 65: 'What is not tolerated at the outset is tolerated during continuance' (يغتفر في البقاء ما لا يغتفر في الابتداء). The author explains that this principle is analogous to the idea that preventing harm is easier than removing it. In contract law, this means that certain defects in conditions that would invalidate a contract at its inception may be overlooked once the contract is ongoing. The Hanafi jurists apply this to several cases. For example, in a lease (ijarah) or gift (hibah) of divisible property, the leased or gifted item must be distinctly identified at the time of the contract; any ambiguity (shuyu') renders the contract invalid. However, if the ambiguity arises later (e.g., due to inheritance or partition), it is tolerated because it occurs during the contract's continuance. Similarly, in a sale of multiple items for a lump sum, buying one item for a share of the price is invalid at inception due to price uncertainty. But if the buyer purchased all items and some are later reclaimed by a third party, the sale of the remaining items remains valid and the buyer can seek restitution from the seller for the price of the reclaimed items. This illustrates that the law facilitates the continuation of contracts and avoids unnecessary disruption.

Key Terms
البقاء أسهل من الابتداء (al-baqā' ashal min al-ibtidā'): A legal maxim meaning that maintaining an existing legal situation is less burdensome than initiating it; therefore, some defects are tolerated during continuance but not at inception.
الشيوع (al-shuyū'): An undivided share in property; ambiguity or co-ownership where the portion is not specified.
البيع بالحصة (al-bay' bi al-hissah): A sale where a buyer purchases one item out of a group for a proportional share of a lump sum price, invalid due to price uncertainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule 'What is not tolerated at the outset is tolerated during continuance', which of the following is correct regarding a lease (ijarah) contract?
A) An undivided share (shuyu') in the leased property at the time of contracting invalidates the lease.
B) An undivided share arising after the contract is formed invalidates the lease.
C) Both initial and subsequent undivided shares are tolerated.
D) Neither initial nor subsequent undivided shares are tolerated.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-baqa' ashal min al-ibtida'' ensures that contracts once validly formed are not easily disrupted by later changes.

Rule Fifteen: The Accessory Follows and Its Applications
Explanation of the maxim 'the accessory follows' with illustrative furuat and a derivative rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-tabi' tabi'' and apply it to identify accessorial rights and obligations in sales, pledges, acknowledgments, and property ownership.

Core Idea
The accessory shares the legal ruling of the principal—what is attached by nature or necessity follows without separate mention.

In Hanafi law, the maxim 'the accessory follows' simplifies transactions by automatically including elements that are part of a thing or necessary for its use. This lesson explains the maxim's scope, illustrates it with practical examples, and derives a sub-rule on ownership of necessities. Mastery of this rule is essential for muftis and jurists handling contracts, property rights, and pledges.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and define 'accessory'
Learn the exact wording of the maxim and the Hanafi definition of an accessory: something that is part of the principal (like a limb, fetus, wool, milk) or necessary for it (like a key to a lock).
2
Apply the maxim to the given furuat
Review each example from the text: sale of sheep (wool included), pregnant cow (fetus included), lock (key included), acknowledgment of sword (scabbard/belt included), and pledge of animal (offspring included). Explain why each item is an accessory.
In each case, the accessory is automatically included even if not mentioned.
3
Derive the sub-rule on ownership of necessities
Connect the maxim to the derived rule: 'Whoever owns something owns what is necessary for it.' This extends the logic to land and shared property rights.
Necessities are not limited to physical parts; they include things essential for the thing's function.
4
Apply the sub-rule to land and shared alley
Understand that ownership of land includes airspace and subsurface, and buying a house in a shared dead-end alley includes a share of the alley as an accessory right.
These applications prevent later disputes over implicit rights.
5
Evaluate the maxim's significance in Islamic law
Recognize how this maxim facilitates trade and reduces litigation by providing default rules for common accessories.
It is a cornerstone of Hanafi transactional law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة عشرة: التابع تابع» (م/٤٧).
أي إن التابع لغيره في الوجود يتبعه في الحكم، فيسري عليه ما يسري على متبوعه والتابع هو ما كان جزءاً من غيره، أو كالجزء في الاتصال الخلقي كالعضو من الحيوان والجنين في بطن أمه، والصوف على الغنم واللبن في الضرع؛ أو كان من ضرورات الشيء كالمفتاح من القفل .
- فلو بيعت شاة دخل في البيع صوفها فليس للبائع جزه. ولو بيعت بقرة حامل دخل جنينها، أو قفل دخل مفتاحه، ولو لم يصرح بذلك في العقد .
- ولو أقر شخص لآخر بملكية سيف متنازع عليه دخل في الإقرار قرابه وحمائله .
- وزوائد الرهن، كولد الدابة المرهونة تبقى في يد المرتهن تبعاً لأصلها .
ما يتفرع عنها من قواعد
٦٧/٨١ - أ - من مَلَك شيئاً ملك ما هو من ضروراته» (م/٤٩).
وعلى هذا قرر الفقهاء :
- أن ملك الأرض يستتبع ملك ما فوقها وما تحتها: فيبني صاحبها الطباق، ويحفر في الأعماق إلى ما يشاء علواً وسفلا (۱).
- وأن من يشتري داراً واقعة في سكة غير نافذة مشتركة بين عدة دور يملك بحكم التبعية حصة الدار من الطريق في هذه السكة ولو لم ينص عليها في العقد.
Rule Fifteen: 'The accessory follows' (art. 47).
Meaning that the accessory to something in existence follows it in legal ruling, so what applies to the principal applies to it. The accessory is that which is part of something else, or like a part in organic connection, such as a limb of an animal, the fetus in its mother's womb, the wool on a sheep, the milk in the udder; or it is among the necessities of something, like the key to a lock.
- If a sheep is sold, its wool is included in the sale, so the seller may not cut it. If a pregnant cow is sold, its fetus is included; or if a lock is sold, its key is included, even if not explicitly stated in the contract.
- If someone acknowledges to another the ownership of a disputed sword, its scabbard and belt are included in the acknowledgment.
- And the accretions of a pledge (rahn), such as the offspring of a pledged animal, remain in the hand of the pledgee as accessory to the principal.

Derived rules:
67/81 – 'Whoever owns something owns what is necessary for it' (art. 49).
Based on this, the jurists decided:
- That ownership of land entails ownership of what is above and below it: so the owner may build floors upward and dig deep downward as high and low as he wishes.
- And that whoever buys a house located in a dead-end alley shared by several houses owns, by virtue of accessory, the house's share of the alley road, even if not stated in the contract.
Source pages p. 957

Commentary
This passage presents Base Rule 15, 'The accessory follows' (التابع تابع), and its derivative Sub-Rule 67, 'Whoever owns something owns what is necessary for it' (من ملك شيئاً ملك ما هو من ضروراته). The maxim establishes that anything attached to a principal thing—whether organically (like a limb, fetus, wool, milk) or by necessity (like a key to a lock)—shares its legal ruling. Thus, when the principal is sold, the accessory is automatically included. For example, selling a sheep includes its wool; selling a pregnant cow includes the unborn calf; selling a lock includes its key—all without explicit mention. Similarly, in a confession (iqrar) regarding a disputed sword, its scabbard and belt are included. In pledge (rahn), the offspring of a pledged animal is accessory and remains with the pledgee. The derivative rule extends this to necessities: owning land gives ownership of the airspace above and ground below for construction. Buying a house in a shared dead-end alley gives the buyer a right to the alley as an accessory right, even if not mentioned. These rules are based on the Hanafi principle that the accessory follows the principal in existence and ruling, facilitating transactions and avoiding unnecessary disputes.

Key Terms
التابع (al-tābi'): The accessory attached to something else, either as a part (like limb) or a necessity (like key), which follows the legal ruling of the principal.
ضرورات الشيء (ḍarūrāt al-shay'): Necessities of a thing without which it cannot function properly, e.g., a key for a lock.
زوائد الرهن (zawā'id al-rahn): Accretions or offspring of a pledged item, which follow the original pledge in ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'The accessory follows', if someone buys a lock, what is included in the sale?
A) Only the lock itself, nothing else.
B) The lock and its key, even if not mentioned.
C) The key only if separately agreed.
D) The lock and any duplicate keys.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-tabi' tabi'' ensures that accessories and necessities follow the principal in legal rulings, simplifying transactions and preventing disputes over implicit inclusions.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate

Applying the Maxim: The Follower Is Not Singled Out
Refining the scope of a legal maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-tābi‘ lā yufradu bil-ḥukm' to identify valid and invalid contracts and distinguish when a follower can be independently ruled upon in tort cases.

Core Idea
A part of something cannot be sold alone; the maxim governs contracts, not liability.

This lesson unpacks a crucial Hanafi legal maxim: the follower cannot be singled out for a ruling. While its wording seems sweeping, its actual application is limited to contracts of sale. You will learn to distinguish between the core scope and the exceptions, especially in tort (ghasb) cases.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its apparent generality
Begin by reciting the maxim: 'al-tābi‘ lā yufradu bil-ḥukm'. Note that its literal wording appears to cover all rulings, but scholars have restricted its scope.
The author warns that the maxim is 'unstable in application' due to this generality.
2
Identify the intended scope: sales contracts
The maxim applies when the 'follower' is a part or like a part of a contracted object. In sales, such a follower cannot be the sole subject of the contract.
Example: selling a limb from a live sheep is invalid, even if later slaughtered.
3
Work through the furuat (branch cases)
Apply the maxim to the given examples: selling a fetus, milk in the udder, or wool on the back. Confirm that each is invalid because the follower is attached and not independently saleable.
Stipulating the exclusion of the fetus as a condition corrupts the sale.
4
Identify the exception: tort liability (ghasb)
Outside contracts, a follower can be subject to independent rulings. For example, the offspring of a usurped animal is not considered usurped, nor is the usurper liable for its natural death.
This exception rests on the principle 'Certainty is not removed by doubt'.
5
Synthesize the maxim's operation for fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, first determine if the case involves a contract or a tort. If contract, the follower cannot be singled out. If tort, the follower may have its own ruling.
The maxim is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) that clarifies the boundary of contractual capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٨/٨١ ـ ب ـ التابع لا يفرد بالحكم» (م/٤٨).
هذه القاعدة مضطربة التطبيق والفروع لأن صيغتها فيما يظهر أعم من : موضوعها، ولذا يذكر لها الشراح مستثنيات كثيرة.
أ - فالمراد بها فيما اعتقد أنّ التابع الذي هو من قبيل الجزء أو كالجزء من غيره لا يصلح أن يكون محلاً في العقود، أي معقوداً عليه بخصوصه :
فصله .
- فبيع عضو من الغنمة وهي حَيَّة باطل ولو ذبحت بعد ذلك وأمكن.
- وبيع جنين الدابة الحامل دون أمه باطل. واستثناؤه من بيع شرطاً مفسداً للبيع.
وكذا شأن اللبن في الضرع والصوف على ظهر الغنم.
ب - أما فيما وراء هذا النطاق فالتابع يمكن أن يُفرد بالأحكام كما في حالة التعدي :
- فإذا ولدت الدابة المغصوبة لا يعتبر ولدها مغصوباً لأن الغاصب ما أزال عنه يد مالك، وإنما حصل في يده حصولاً (ر: ف (٩/٢٣) وكذا لو هلك بلا تعد منه أو تقصير لا يضمنه ولكن لو أتلفه هو يضمنه .
68/81 - b: 'The follower is not singled out for a ruling' (M/48). This maxim is unstable in application and branches because its wording appears more general than its scope. Therefore commentators mention many exceptions. a - In my belief, the intended meaning is that the follower, which is of the type of a part or like a part of another, is not suitable to be a subject in contracts, i.e., contracted upon specifically. So selling a limb from a live sheep is invalid, even if it is slaughtered afterwards and possible. And selling the fetus of a pregnant animal without its mother is invalid. Stipulating its exclusion from the sale is a corrupting condition. The same applies to milk in the udder and wool on the back of sheep. b - Beyond this scope, the follower can be singled out for rulings, as in the case of transgression: If the offspring is born from a usurped (ghasb) animal, the offspring is not considered usurped because the usurper did not remove it from the owner's hand, but it came into his hand by occurrence. Similarly, if it perishes without his transgression or negligence, he does not guarantee it. But if he destroys it, he guarantees it.
Source pages p. 959

Commentary
This passage explains the second maxim 'The follower is not singled out for a ruling' from the Majalla. The author notes that this maxim is problematic because its wording is broader than its actual application. He clarifies that the intended scope is that a follower (which is a part or like a part) cannot be the independent subject of a contract. For example, selling a limb from a live sheep is invalid, even if the sheep is later slaughtered and the limb could be sold separately. Similarly, selling a fetus without its mother is invalid; making the fetus an exception in a sale of the mother is a corrupting condition. The same applies to milk in the udder or wool on the sheep: they cannot be sold separately while still attached. However, outside contracts, a follower can be subject to other rulings, such as liability for usurpation (ghasb). If someone usurps a pregnant animal and it gives birth, the offspring is not considered usurped because it was not in the owner's possession; the usurper is not liable if it dies naturally, but is liable if he destroys it. This exception shows that the maxim applies mainly to contracts, not to torts.

Key Terms
التابع (al-tābi‘): A part or like a part of another, which cannot be the sole object of a contract.
التعدي (al-ta‘addī): Transgression, usurpation (ghasb) causing liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid if the buyer and seller agree on a price.
B) Invalid because the milk is a follower and cannot be sold separately.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'The follower is not singled out for a ruling' applies primarily to sales contracts, not to torts like usurpation.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

If a Thing is Void, What is Within It is Also Void
Explains a maxim with applications and exceptions regarding voidness of contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'إذا بطل الشيء بطل ما في ضمنه' to identify voided subsidiary obligations in contracts and recognize exceptions based on the purpose of the law.

Core Idea
The voidness of a primary contract voids all its subsidiary terms, but the purpose of the law may override this maxim.

This maxim, codified in Majalla article 52, is a cornerstone of Hanafi contract theory: the subsidiary follows the primary. But as with all qawaid, there are istithna'at driven by maqasid. This lesson unpacks the maxim, its applications in sales and settlements, and the key exception in preemption (shuf'a), where the purpose of repelling harm takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its corollary
Write the maxim in Arabic: 'إذا بطل الشيء بطل ما في ضمنه' and its equivalent: 'إذا بطل المتضمن بطل المتضمن'. Understand the basic meaning: if the primary thing is void, the subsidiary is also void.
The corollary switches active/passive voice but conveys the same rule.
2
Identify the scope of the maxim
Recognize that this maxim applies to all contracts and transactions. It governs the fate of conditions, obligations, and terms that are subsidiary to the primary contract.
The scope is general (kulliya) but not absolute – it has exceptions.
3
Work through the furuat (applications)
Analyze the examples: (1) void sale voids its conditions; (2) void settlement (sulh) voids the transfer of consideration; (3) void sale due to istihqaq voids the built-upon release (ibra). For each, trace how the maxim applies.
Note that the author extends the maxim to include 'what is built upon' the void thing.
4
Identify the istithna' (exception)
Examine the shuf'a case: a preemptor settles his right for a consideration, but the settlement is void; however, the preemption right still drops without compensation. Understand why the maxim does not fully apply.
The exception is based on istihsan and the purpose (maqsad) of shuf'a – to remove harm, not to be exploited.
5
Determine the overriding principle
Recognize that the exception is governed by the qawida 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (Acts are judged by intentions). When the maxim would contradict the underlying purpose, the purpose prevails.
This illustrates the hierarchical relationship between qawaid.

Source Text
