Chapter 81: Explanation of the Majalla's Maxims and Their Classification. ... his brother, so he shares that person's share of the father's inheritance. 72/81 - And - 'If a thing is void, what is within it is also void' (M/52). Similar to it is their statement: 'If the containing thing is void, the contained thing is void' (with the active voice in the first and the passive voice in the second). So if a contract becomes void, its conditions and obligations become void, because they are subsidiary to it. And if a person settles with his opponent who claims against him for a consideration, then the claimant admits after the settlement that he had no right against him, the settlement becomes void, so the consideration given in the settlement is void, and the settlor has the right to reclaim it. And just as the content of a contract becomes void with its voidness, so also what is built upon it becomes void. So if two parties make a sale and complete the exchange of the sold item and the price, then each releases the other from every right and claim related to this sale, then the sold item is reclaimed from the buyer's possession, the buyer recovers the price from the seller, because when the sale became void due to the repossession of the sold item, the release built upon it became void. Based on this, the text of the maxim should state: 'void is what is within it and what is built upon it.' Exceptions: This maxim has exceptions, among them: if a preemption claimant settles for the right of preemption in a sold property for a consideration, the settlement is not valid, and his preemption right drops without consideration, because the right of preemption is legislated to be exercised actively to repel an undesirable neighbor, not to be exploited exploitatively. So the settlement here became void, but what is within it (the dropping of preemption) did not become void, because ...
Source pages p. 962

Commentary
This passage discusses the maxim 'If a thing is void, what is within it is also void' (Majalla article 52) and its corollary 'If the containing thing is void, the contained thing is void.' The author first explains the direct application: voidness of a contract voids its terms and obligations. An example is given of a settlement (sulh) where the claimant later admits he had no claim; the settlement is void, and the consideration must be returned. Then the passage extends the maxim to cover what is 'built upon' the void thing: if a sale is rescinded due to a defect in title (istihqaq), the mutual release (ibra) that was part of the sale is also void. The author suggests the maxim should therefore include both 'what is within' and 'what is built upon'. Finally, an exception is noted: in preemption (shuf'a), if the preemptor settles his right for a consideration, the settlement is void, but the preemption right still drops (without compensation). This is because preemption is meant to remove harm, not to be a source of profit. The exception shows that the maxim does not apply when its application would contradict the underlying purpose of the law.

Key Terms
ما في ضمنه (ma fi dimnihi): What is within it; the subsidiary obligations, conditions, or rights that are part of the primary contract.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception; a case where the maxim does not apply due to a specific legal reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The preemption right remains and the consideration is returned.
B) The preemption right drops but the consideration is not returned.
C) The preemption right is retained and the consideration is forfeited.
D) The settlement is void but the dropping of preemption is effective without compensation.

Key Takeaway
The voidness of a primary contract voids its subsidiary elements, but the purpose of the law may carve out exceptions, as exemplified in shuf'a.

Maxim: Sharia Permission Negates Liability | الجواز الشرعي ينافي الضمان
Explaining and qualifying the maxim with furuat and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-jawaz al-shar'i yunafi al-daman' to distinguish between absolute and restricted permission and state the resulting liability rulings.

Core Idea
Sharia permission removes liability only when absolute; restricted permission preserves liability.

This passage introduces the twenty-second major Hanafi legal maxim: 'Sharia permission negates liability'. At first glance, the maxim seems to say that any permissible act absolves the actor from financial liability for resultant harm. However, a closer reading reveals a crucial qualification: only absolute (mutlaq) permission negates liability; restricted (muqayyad) permission does not. Understanding this distinction is essential for a mufti when determining liability in a wide range of cases, from property damage to hire contracts.

Guided Path
1
Define the core maxim and daman
Read the author's definition of daman (financial compensation for harm) and the basic statement of the maxim. Identify the underlying rationale: Sharia permission implies removal of responsibility.
2
Examine the furuat (branches) for absolute permission
Study the three pairs of examples: digging on private vs public property, loading a hired animal normally vs overloading, and the finder withholding with vs without expenses. Note that in each pair, the first action (with absolute permission) incurs no liability, while the second (transgression) does.
These pairs illustrate the maxim in action.
3
Identify the qualification: absolute vs restricted permission
The author explicitly states that the maxim applies only to al-jawaz al-mutlaq (absolute permission). Restricted permission (al-jawaz al-muqayyad) does not negate liability. Locate this distinction in the text.
This is the key analytical move.
4
Analyze the apparent exceptions as cases of restricted permission
The text reinterprets two common exceptions: the mudtarr (compelled person) eating another's food and the rider/driver of an animal causing damage. Show that in both cases, the permission is restricted by conditions (preserving others' rights, safety). Therefore they are not true exceptions but applications of the refined maxim.
This reduces the number of exceptions.
5
Restate the maxim correctly
The author concludes that the maxim should be phrased as: 'Absolute Sharia permission negates liability.' Confirm that the refined version clarifies the scope and eliminates ambiguity.
Memorize the refined formulation.

80/81 - The Twenty-Second Rule: Sharia Permission Negates Liability. (91/p) Ad-daman: is the obligation of financial compensation for harm to others. The meaning of this rule is that no liability is incurred by a person for his action or inaction if it is permissible (ja'iz) according to Sharia, because the Lawgiver's permission for that act or omission implies removal of responsibility for it; otherwise it would not be permissible. Based on this there are many branches: - Among them is if a person digs a pit on his own private property, and someone else's animal falls into it, he is not liable because his digging is permissible. In contrast, if he digs in the public road, he then transgresses the right of the public, so he is liable for the damage. - Likewise, if a lessee loads a hired animal to the customary extent and it perishes, he is not liable because his action is permissible. But if he loads it more than customary, he is liable. - If a finder of a lost item (multaqit) refuses to hand it over to its owner until he takes the expenses he spent on it with the judge's permission, and the lost item perishes in his hand, he is not liable for it, nor does his right to expenses lapse with its perishing, because his refusal is permissible. But if he had no expenses on it, then he becomes a transgressor by withholding, so he is liable. This rule, it appears, is restricted to permission that is absolute (mutlaq); if the permission is restricted (muqayyad), it does not negate liability. - Therefore, one who is compelled (mudtarr) must pay the value of another's food if he eats it to save himself from destruction, as previously mentioned (see vol. 81, p. 34) that his eating is obligatory (wajib), not merely permissible. This is because this permission is restricted by Sharia to preserving the rights of others. - Likewise, the driver and rider of an animal on the public road are liable for what it destroys with its legs or mouth. This is because walking on the public road, although permissible, is restricted by Sharia to the condition of safety in everything that can be guarded against (1) (from the commentary of my father, may Allah have mercy on him, on the Rules with modification and clarification). With this derivation, the exceptions to this rule are reduced; the commentators count these two branches and their like as exceptions to it. Therefore, the rule should be stated: 'Absolute Sharia permission negates liability.'
Source pages p. 970

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-second major legal maxim: 'Sharia permission negates liability' (al-jawaz al-shar'i yunafi al-daman). The author first defines daman as financial compensation for harm. He then explains the rational basis: if the Lawgiver has permitted an act, it implies that no responsibility for resultant harm attaches to the actor; otherwise the act would not be truly permissible. Several applications (furu) illustrate the rule: digging a pit on private property incurs no liability for resulting damage, but digging on a public road does; loading a hired animal normally incurs no liability but overloading does; a finder of lost property who withholds it to recover expenses is not liable for its loss, but unjustified withholding makes him liable. The author then notes that this rule is qualified: it applies only to absolute (mutlaq) permission, not restricted (muqayyad) permission. He gives two examples often cited as exceptions: the case of necessity (mudtarr) eating another's food is obligatory, not merely permissible, and is restricted by the condition of preserving rights, so liability remains; similarly, driving an animal on a public road is permissible but restricted by the condition of safety from harm that can be avoided, so liability attaches. The author concludes that by this analysis, the exceptions are fewer than often claimed, and the maxim should be phrased as 'Absolute Sharia permission negates liability'.

Key Terms
الضمان (ad-daman): Obligation of financial compensation for harm to another
الجواز الشرعي المطلق (al-jawaz ash-shar'i al-mutlaq): An act permitted without any restrictive condition
الجواز الشرعي المقيد (al-jawaz ash-shar'i al-muqayyad): An act permitted but with a condition attached by Sharia

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, why does a person who digs a pit on his private property not owe compensation if an animal falls in?
A) Because the animal should have been careful
B) Because the digging is permissible, so liability is negated
C) Because the pit is on private property and no one should enter
D) Because the owner of the animal is responsible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-jawaz al-shar'i al-mutlaq yunafi al-daman' is a powerful tool for determining liability: absolute permission removes liability, restricted permission preserves it.

Profit with Liability (الخراج بالضمان)
Explaining the maxim that profit (usufruct) is with liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' to determine entitlement to profits in sales and leases, and identify the underlying hadith.

Core Idea
Profit follows liability: the one who bears the risk of loss deserves the benefit.

This lesson introduces one of the great maxims of Islamic fiqh, known as 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'. The maxim is derived directly from a prophetic hadith and governs the relationship between risk and reward in contracts. Understanding this principle is essential for a mufti when adjudicating disputes over usufruct and liability.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terms
Identify the two key terms: 'al-kharaj' (profit/usufruct) and 'ad-daman' (liability/risk). Recognize that kharaj includes benefits like fruits or hire, and daman refers to the obligation to compensate for loss.
This maxim is often paired with 'al-ghunm bi al-ghurm' (gain accompanies risk).
2
Memorize the hadith source
Learn that the maxim is a Prophetic hadith narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi (who authenticated it), al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and al-Hakim. Commit the maxim's wording and its status.
Al-Tirmidhi's authentication (sahih) gives it strong evidentiary weight.
3
Apply the maxim to a sale scenario
Consider a buyer who purchases a fruit-bearing tree before the fruit is harvested. The fruit ripens while still with the seller. The maxim rules the fruit belongs to the seller because he bore the risk of loss (daman).
Risk (damn) here includes responsibility for destruction or damage.
4
Apply the maxim to a lease scenario
Consider a person who hires out an animal. Before delivery to the hirer, the animal is used for transport and earns a fee. The maxim gives the fee to the owner (lessor) because the risk remained with him.
The same applies to any usufruct (manfa'ah) before possession transfers.
5
Identify the scope and authority
Recognize that this maxim is a kulliya (universal qawida) governing all contracts where risk and profit are separable. It is recorded in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and relied upon by Hanafi muftis.
The maxim yields to explicit contractual stipulations unless they contravene shariah.

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-third major maxim: 'Profit with liability' (al-kharaj bi al-daman). The author defines kharaj as the yield or benefit derived from a thing, such as the fruits of a tree or the hire of an animal. Daman was defined in the previous rule as financial compensation. The maxim is based directly on a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) narrated by Ahmad, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi (who authenticated it), al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, and al-Hakim. The reference to al-Durr al-Mukhtar indicates its application in Hanafi fiqh, specifically in the context of liability for damage caused by animals. The maxim means that the right to benefit from a property belongs to the one who bears the risk of loss; for example, if a buyer purchases a tree and it bears fruit before delivery, the fruit belongs to the seller because the risk of loss was on him. This principle is widely applied in sales, leases, and other contracts.

الضمان (ad-daman): Liability for loss or compensation

The Maxim al-Kharāj bi al-Ḍamān and Its Scope
Explains the legal maxim linking profit to liability, with scope and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān' to determine which profits a liable possessor may retain and distinguish the exceptions for generated increments and unlawful possession.

Core Idea
Profit is in exchange for liability — but only for non-generated benefits from lawful possession.

This lesson unpacks the twenty-third maxim from al-Qawā‘id al-Kulliyya, rooted in a prophetic hadith. The maxim ties the entitlement to profits with the risk of loss, but the text carefully delimits its application, excluding generated increments and cases of unlawful possession. Understanding this maxim is essential for correctly allocating benefits in sales, leases, and other transactions.

Guided Path
1
1. State the Maxim and its Source
Identify the Arabic text 'al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān' and note that it is based on a sahīh hadith reported by Ahmad, the four Sunan, and al-Tirmidhī (1285). Understand the incident: a buyer of a defective slave was allowed to keep profits because he bore the risk of loss.
The hadith is a primary source; the maxim is considered a wusta (intermediate) rule.
2
2. Define Key Terms
Kharāj: the yield or benefit (usufruct, hire). Ḍamān: liability for loss/destruction. The maxim means profit is earned in exchange for bearing the risk.
These definitions are from the passage.
3
3. Identify the Scope: Non-Generated Benefits from Lawful Possession
The author specifies that 'kharāj' here refers to non-generated benefits (like rent, usufruct) arising from a lawful cause (e.g., valid purchase). Generated increments (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) are governed by a different maxim: 'al-tawallud min al-mamlūk sabab al-milkiyya' (ownership of generated things belongs to the owner of the original).
This distinction reduces apparent exceptions to the maxim.
4
4. Apply to the Cases in the Text
Work through the furū‘: (a) buyer of defective slave keeps profits (non-generated, lawful possession). (b) seller before delivery does not own offspring/milk even though he bears risk—because generated increments follow ownership. (c) usurper does not keep profits even though liable—because possession is unlawful.
Apply the maxim and its exceptions step by step.
5
5. Recognize the Underlying Principle: Absolute vs. Restricted Permission
The author notes that when permission to possess is absolute (e.g., by valid contract), liability negates claims to profit? Actually the maxim already gives profit to the liable party. But if permission is restricted (e.g., eating food to save life, riding on public road), liability is not negated. However, for the maxim ‘profit in exchange for liability’, the key is that the possession must be lawful (i.e., not usurpation).
This ties to the preceding rule about legal permission negating liability.

Source Text

والخراج المقصود بهذا الحكم فيما يظهر هو ما كان غير متولد، وذلك كالمنافع والأجرة على أن يكون أيضاً مستنداً إلى حالة مشروعة، كالشراء، وفقاً لما يدل عليه مورد الحديث (١).

Chapter Ten: Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh. He spent it with the judge's permission, then the lost property perished in his hand; he is not liable for it, and its maintenance is not forfeited by its destruction, because his refusal to spend was permissible. But if he had no maintenance on it, then he is aggressor by the refusal and is liable. This rule, apparently, is conditioned by the permission being absolute; if the permission is restricted, it does not negate liability. Therefore, the person in dire necessity guarantees the value of another's food if he eats it to save himself from destruction, as previously (ref: f 34/81) that eating it is obligatory, not merely permissible, because this permissibility is legally restricted to preserving the rights of others. Likewise, the leader of the beast and its rider on the public road are liable for what it damages with its legs or mouth, because walking on the public road, although permissible, is legally restricted by the condition of safety in everything that can be avoided. (1) From my father's commentary on the rules adapted and clarified. By this derivation, the exceptions to this rule are reduced; the commentators count these two branches and their like among its exceptions. Therefore, it should have been said in the rule: 'Absolute legal permission negates liability.' 1/81 - The Twenty-Third Rule: Profit is in exchange for liability (M/85). 'Kharāj' of a thing is the yield obtained from it, such as benefits of the thing and the hire of an animal; 'liability' was explained in the previous rule. This rule is a text of a prophetic hadith reported by Ahmad in his Musnad, the authors of the four Sunan, al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak, and authenticated by al-Tirmidhi (1285) in the Book of Sales. Its meaning: that entitlement to the yield is caused by bearing liability, i.e., bearing the risk of destruction. So the benefits and yield of a thing are entitled by the one who bears the loss of destruction of that thing if it perishes; thus entitlement to the fruit is in return for bearing the loss. This hadith occurred in an incident: a person bought a slave and used him for a time, then found an old defect. He disputed with the seller to the Prophet (peace be upon him), who ruled that he be returned to the seller. He said: O Messenger of Allah, he has used my slave! So he (peace be upon him) said: 'Profit is in exchange for liability.' (Ref: Book of Judgments of the Messenger of Allah by al-Qurtubi in Sales, p. 76). The 'kharāj' meant by this ruling, apparently, is that which is not generated, such as benefits and hire, and also that it must be based on a legitimate condition, like purchase, according to what the context of the hadith indicates (1). As for generated increments, like the offspring of an animal, its milk, wool, and fruit of trees, another legal rule governs them, making them belong to the owner of the origin from which they are generated, without regard to liability or its absence, because one of the causes of ownership is generation from owned property as previously mentioned (ref: f 9/23). Therefore, if a usurped animal gives birth in the usurper's possession, its offspring belongs to the owner, not the usurper, even though the liability for its destruction is on the usurper. Likewise, if an animal sold gives birth with the seller before delivery to the buyer, its offspring and milk belong to the buyer, and the seller may not benefit from it or rent it out, even though the liability for destruction of the sold item before delivery to the buyer is on the seller. By this interpretation of 'kharāj' and its connection with liability, such (1) If the liability is compensatory due to an illegitimate cause like usurpation, it does not entitle the guarantor to the yield of the guaranteed thing. Hence, the usurper is not permitted the benefits of the usurped item even if he is liable for it.
Source pages p. 971

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal maxim 'al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān' (profit is in exchange for liability), which is the twenty-third rule in the author's enumeration. The author begins by finishing a prior rule about absolute permission negating liability, then transitions to this maxim. The maxim is derived from a prophetic hadith authenticated by al-Tirmidhi. The incident: a man bought a slave, used him, then found a defect; the Prophet ruled the slave could be returned but the buyer could keep the profits because he bore the risk of loss during his possession. The author clarifies that 'kharāj' here refers to non-generated benefits like usufruct and hire, provided the possession is legitimate (e.g., purchase). Generated increments (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) follow a different rule: they belong to the owner of the original property, regardless of who bears liability, as ownership arises from generation from owned property. Examples: a usurper does not own the offspring of a usurped animal because the original owner retains ownership, even though the usurper is liable for the animal. Similarly, a seller who still possesses a sold animal before delivery does not own its offspring, even though he bears the risk of destruction until delivery. The passage also notes that if liability is compensatory due to an illegal act like usurpation, the rule does not apply—the usurper cannot keep profits. This explanation reduces apparent exceptions to the maxim.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharāj): The yield or benefit obtained from a thing, such as usufruct and hire
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability for loss or destruction of property, bearing the risk of loss
المتولدات (al-mutawallidāt): Generated increments such as offspring, milk, wool, fruit that come into being from the original property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān', if a person buys a sheep, uses its wool, then discovers a defect and returns it, may he keep the wool?
A) Yes, because he bore the risk of loss during his possession.
B) No, because the wool is a generated increment belonging to the seller.
C) Yes, because the wool is a non-generated profit.
D) No, because the maxim only applies to hire and similar benefits.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-kharāj bi al-ḍamān' entitles a lawful possessor to non-generated profits in exchange for bearing risk, but generated increments and unlawful possession are exceptions.

The Maxim Al-Kharaj bi al-Daman: Profit with Liability
Defining the maxim with scope and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' to identify who is entitled to profits in a transaction, and distinguish it from the separate rule governing generated additions.

Core Idea
Profit belongs to the one who bears the risk of loss, but generated additions belong to the owner of the origin.

This lesson unpacks one of the great Hanafi legal maxims—'al-kharaj bi al-daman'—which links profit to liability. You will learn its textual basis, scope, and the critical exceptions that prevent misapplication. Understanding this maxim is essential for correctly assigning benefits in sales, leases, and unlawful possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its literal meaning
State the maxim in Arabic and English: 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' = profit is with liability. Define each term: kharaj (profit/yield) and daman (liability/risk).
2
Trace the proof from the hadith
The hadith from al-Qurtubi narrates a buyer who used a slave, found a defect, and returned it. The Prophet (ﷺ) ruled the buyer keeps the profits because the seller bore the risk before sale. This establishes the maxim.
The exact wording 'الخراج بالضمان' is quoted.
3
Understand the definition of 'kharaj' in the maxim
Kharaj here refers only to non-generated benefits (e.g., rent, usufruct) arising from a lawful transaction (e.g., purchase). Generated additions (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) are excluded.
The footnote clarifies that liability from unlawful acts (e.g., usurpation) does not entitle the liable party to profit.
4
Apply the maxim to furuat (derived cases)
Work through the two furuat from the text: (1) Buyer uses item, then finds defect → buyer keeps profits. (2) Sold animal gives birth before delivery → offspring belongs to buyer (but not by this maxim, by a separate rule).
Note that the second furu is actually an exception, but the text explains it is outside the maxim's scope.
5
Distinguish the exceptions
List the two istithna'at: (a) Generated additions belong to owner of origin, governed by 'التولد من المملوك سبب الملكية'. (b) Unlawful liability (usurpation) does not make profit permissible for the liable party.
These exceptions are crucial to avoid misapplication.

Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and their Classification. Its meaning: The entitlement to profit (kharaj) is caused by bearing liability (daman), i.e., bearing the consequence of destruction. So the benefits and yield of a thing are entitled to the one who bears the loss of destruction of that thing if it were destroyed, thus entitlement to the fruit is in return for bearing the loss. This hadith occurred in an incident where a person bought a slave and used him for a time, then found an old defect. He complained to the Messenger (peace be upon him) and he judged its return to the seller. He said: O Messenger of Allah, he has utilized my slave! He (peace be upon him) said: "Profit is with liability" (ref: Book of Judgments of the Messenger by al-Qurtubi in Buyu, p. 76). The profit intended by this ruling appears to be what is non-generated (ghayr mutawallid), such as benefits and rent, and also based on a lawful situation, like purchase, according to the context of the hadith (1). As for generated additions, like the offspring of an animal, its milk, wool, and fruit of a tree, another legal rule governs them, making them belong to the owner of the origin from which they are generated, without regard to liability or its absence, because one of the causes of ownership is generation from owned property, as previously stated (see 9/23). Therefore, if a usurped animal gives birth with the usurper, the offspring belongs to the owner, not the usurper, even though the liability for its destruction is on the usurper. Likewise, if an animal that has been sold gives birth with the seller before delivery to the buyer, the offspring and milk belong to the buyer, and the seller may not benefit from it or rent it, while the liability for destruction of the sold item before delivery to the buyer is on the seller. With this interpretation of kharaj and its relationship to daman, such rulings are not problematic for the rule or exceptions from it, because they are outside the intended meaning of the hadith text. (1) If the liability is compensatory due to an unlawful cause like usurpation, it does not make the profit of the liable item permissible for the guarantor. Hence, the benefits of a usurped item are not permissible for the usurper even if he is liable for it.
Source pages p. 972

Commentary
This passage explains the famous Hanafi maxim "al-kharaj bi al-daman" (profit is with liability). The author first defines it: entitlement to profit or yield arises from bearing the risk of destruction. The proof is a hadith where a buyer who used a slave then discovered a defect was allowed to keep the profits because the seller bore the risk before the sale was finalized. The author then clarifies that "kharaj" here refers only to non-generated benefits (e.g., rent, usufruct) arising from a lawful transaction like purchase. Generated additions (offspring, milk, wool, fruit) are governed by a separate principle: they belong to the owner of the original asset regardless of liability, because generation from owned property is a cause of ownership. Thus, if a usurped animal gives birth, the offspring goes to the rightful owner, not the usurper despite the usurper's liability. Similarly, if a sold animal gives birth before delivery, the offspring belongs to the buyer, not the seller, even though the seller bears the risk until delivery. The footnote emphasizes that if liability is imposed due to unlawful acts like usurpation, the profit does not become permissible for the usurper; thus a usurper cannot benefit from the property even though he is liable.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): The profit, yield, or benefit derived from a thing; specifically non-generated benefits such as rent or usufruct.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability or responsibility for loss or destruction; bearing the risk of harm to the thing.
الزائد المتولد (al-za'id al-mutawallid): Generated additions that are produced from the original asset, such as offspring, milk, wool, or fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The seller, because he bears the risk until delivery
B) The buyer, because the profit is with liability
C) The buyer, because generated additions belong to the owner of the origin
D) The seller, because he still owns the animal until delivery

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' assigns non-generated profits to the party bearing the risk in a lawful transaction, but generated additions follow ownership of the origin, not liability.

The Maxim Al-Ghurm bi al-Ghunm: Loss with Gain
Introducing the converse maxim that loss accompanies gain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' to determine who bears the expense in Hanafi transactional scenarios.

Core Idea
الغرم بالغنم: The one who gains must bear the loss.

This lesson presents the wusta maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm', the converse of the well-known 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'. While the latter states that profit accompanies liability, this maxim asserts that loss accompanies gain. In Hanafi fiqh, this principle governs the allocation of expenses arising from ownership, use, or benefit. By working through the furuat provided by the author, you will learn to identify the beneficiary in any transaction and assign the corresponding costs accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the maxim and its rank
Read the maxim in Arabic (الغرم بالغنم) and its English translation ('Loss is with gain'). Note that it is classified as a wusta (intermediate) maxim and a kulliya (universal) maxim governing obligations and expenses in transactions.
Contrast it immediately with its parallel 'الخراج بالضمان'.
2
Identify the illa (effective cause) in each furu
For each application, ask: who benefits from the transaction or arrangement? The maxim states that the beneficiary must bear the associated loss or cost. The illa is the presence of benefit (ghunm).
Do not confuse benefit with mere possession; e.g., a depositor benefits from the safekeeping, not the borrower.
3
Work through the five furuat in order
Take each case from the text: (1) return of borrowed item – borrower benefits, so pays; (2) return of deposit – owner benefits, so pays; (3) sales contract – buyer benefits, so pays; (4) joint property – each partner benefits proportionately, so pays in proportion; (5) foundling – the public treasury eventually benefits from the estate, so bears expenses. Confirm how each case illustrates the maxim.
4
Note the absence of istithna'at
The author does not list any exceptions to this maxim in the given passage. Understand that the maxim applies broadly, but in other chapters there may be cases where the benefit is not tied to the loss due to a different governing maxim (e.g., al-ghurm is not incurred if the benefit is not realized).
For this lesson, assume general applicability.
5
Relate the maxim to its parallel and apply to a new case
Compare 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' with 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'. Both link benefit and liability, but from opposite directions. Then create a new scenario: a person rents a car and it breaks down due to normal wear; who pays? The renter benefits during the rental, so per the maxim, the renter would bear the repair cost unless the contract states otherwise.
Always consider contractual stipulations that may shift the default rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۲/۸۱ - القاعدة الرابعة والعشرون: الغُرم بالغُنْم (م/۸۷ ) .
هذه القاعدة تعبر عن عكس القاعدة السابقة الخراج بالضمان»، فتفيد أن الضمان أيضاً بالخراج أي أن التكاليف والخسارة التي تحصل من الشيء تكون على من يستفيد منه شرعاً:
فنفقة رد العارية إلى المعير يلتزم بها المستعير بخلاف رد الوديعة، فإن كلفته على المالك المودع، لأن الإيداع لمصلحته.
وأجرة كتابة صك المبايعة على المشتري، لأنها توثيق لانتقال الملكية إليه .
ونفقة تعمير الملك المشترك وترميمه على الشركاء بنسبة حصصهم .
وبيت المال يتحمل نفقة اللقيط، وهو الطفل المنبوذ المجهول النسب، حيث تعود أيضاً تركته إلى بيت المال إذا مات .
82/81 - The twenty-fourth maxim: Loss is with gain (M/87). This maxim expresses the opposite of the previous maxim 'Profit is with liability', indicating that liability is also with profit, i.e., the costs and loss that occur from a thing are borne by the one who benefits from it according to Sharia: The expenses of returning a borrowed item to the lender are borne by the borrower, unlike returning a trust (wadi'ah), whose cost is on the owner-depositor because the deposit is for his benefit. The fee for writing a sales contract is on the buyer, because it documents the transfer of ownership to him. The expense of maintaining and repairing jointly owned property is on the partners in proportion to their shares. The public treasury bears the expense of a foundling (laqit), an abandoned child of unknown lineage, since his estate also reverts to the treasury if he dies.
Source pages p. 973

Commentary
This maxim is the converse of 'al-kharaj bi al-daman'. It states that liability or loss follows benefit (gain). The author provides applications: The borrower must pay the cost of returning a borrowed item because he benefits from the loan; but for a deposit (wadi'ah), the owner pays because the deposit is for his benefit. The buyer pays for writing the sale contract because ownership transfers to him. Joint owners share repair costs proportionally. The state treasury supports a foundling because ultimately the estate of the foundling goes to the treasury. These examples illustrate that whoever enjoys the benefit must bear the associated expenses.

Key Terms
الغرم (al-ghurm): Loss, expense, or financial burden; the costs or damages incurred.
الغنم (al-ghunm): Gain, profit, or benefit; the advantage derived from a thing or transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm', who bears the cost of returning a borrowed item?
A) The lender, because he owns the item
B) The borrower, because he benefited from it
C) Both equally
D) The state

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' allocates costs to the beneficiary, ensuring fairness in Hanafi transactional rulings.

Al-Ni'mah bi Qadr al-Niqmah: Benefit Equal to Burden
Stating a synonymous maxim to the previous two

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah' and identify its relationship to the maxims 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' and 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm'.

Core Idea
Blessing and affliction are proportional — this maxim restates two others.

This short passage introduces the twenty-fifth maxim of al-Zarqa's collection. It is essentially a two-sided formulation that combines two well-known Hanafi maxims into a single balanced statement. Understanding it as a restatement helps you see the unity behind these principles.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim's Arabic phrasing
Learn the exact Arabic: 'النعمة بقدر النقمة، والنقمة بقدر النعمة'. Note the parallel structure.
This is a memorization anchor — precision matters.
2
Identify the two halves and their synonyms
The first half (النعمة بقدر النقمة) is synonymous with 'الخراج بالضمان'. The second half (النقمة بقدر النعمة) is synonymous with 'الغرم بالغنم'.
Each half expresses the same proportionality from a different starting point.
3
Connect to the underlying legal principle
The maxim reflects the Hanafi rule that benefit and liability are inseparable: whoever enjoys a benefit must bear any associated loss, and vice versa.
This principle governs many transactions and bailment cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸٣/۸١ - القاعدة الخامسة والعشرون: النعمة بقدر النقمة، والنقمة بقدر النعمة» (م/ ۸۸).
الجملة الأولى من هذه القاعدة ترادف قاعدة: الخراج بالضمان .
والجملة الثانية منها ترادف القاعدة السابقة: «الغرم بالغنم».
83/81 - The twenty-fifth maxim: 'The blessing is in proportion to the affliction, and the affliction is in proportion to the blessing' (M/88). The first sentence of this maxim is synonymous with the maxim 'Profit is with liability.' The second sentence is synonymous with the previous maxim: 'Loss is with gain.'
Source pages p. 973

Commentary
This very brief passage presents a maxim that is essentially a restatement of the two previous maxims. 'Al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah' (blessing is proportional to affliction) parallels 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit with liability), while 'al-niqmah bi qadr al-ni'mah' (affliction is proportional to blessing) parallels 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (loss with gain). Thus, this maxim reinforces the same principle from both directions.

Key Terms
النعمة (al-ni'mah): Blessing or benefit; used synonymously with profit (kharaj) or gain (ghunm).
النقمة (al-niqmah): Affliction or burden; used synonymously with liability (daman) or loss (ghurm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The maxim 'al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah' is synonymous with which other maxim?
A) Al-ghurm bi al-ghunm
B) Al-kharaj bi al-daman
C) Al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an
D) Al-umur bi maqasidiha

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah' reinforces that benefit and burden are proportionate, synonymous with 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' and 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm'.

The Maxim: Wage and Liability Do Not Combine
Defining a Hanafi-specific maxim on wage and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the Hanafi maxim 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an', identify its scope in contracts of ijarah and ariyah, and apply it to determine liability of a hirer or borrower.

Core Idea
If you pay rent, you are not liable for the item's value — wage and liability cannot coexist.

In the Hanafi school, a key maxim governs the relationship between payment for benefit and liability for the substance: 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an'. This maxim refines our understanding of who bears the risk in rental and loan contracts. It is a specifically Hanafi rule, not accepted by the majority of other schools. This lesson dissects its meaning, scope, and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Define the two key terms
Identify 'ajr' as compensation for usufruct (manfa'ah) for a period, and 'daman' as liability to pay the value of the thing used (qimat al-'ayn) if destroyed. Note that these are distinct: ajr is for the benefit, daman for the substance.
2
State the maxim and its implication
The maxim states that these two cannot combine on the same person simultaneously. If someone receives ajr (e.g., rent), they cannot also be held liable for daman of the same item during the period of hire.
The key implication: a hirer who pays rent is not liable for accidental destruction of the item.
3
Identify the scope of application
The maxim primarily applies in contracts of ijarah (hire/rent) and ariyah (loan) where the hirer/borrower pays an ajr. It determines whether the burden of loss falls on the owner or the user.
Note that in ariyah (loan), there is no ajr, so the maxim does not directly apply; but analogy extends it.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The passage explicitly states this maxim is not adopted by the majority of other schools (Jumhur). This creates a practical ikhtilaf: in Hanafi, the hirer is not liable; in other schools, the hirer may be liable even if paying rent.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf, and the mufti must follow the Hanafi position.

Source Text

الأجر بدل المنفعة عن مدة ما. والضمان المقصود هنا هو الالتزام بقيمة العين المنتفع بها (ر : ف (۸۰/۸۱) ) .
وهي قاعدة حنفية النسب لا يتبناها جمهور المذاهب الفقهية الأخرى. عند الحنفية تتصل اتصالاً وثيقاً بنظريتهم المشهورة في عدم ضمان
84/8 - The twenty-sixth maxim: 'Wage and liability do not combine' (M/86). Wage is the compensation for benefit for a certain period. Liability intended here is the commitment to pay the value of the thing used (see 80/81). This maxim is Hanafi in origin and is not adopted by the majority of other schools of jurisprudence. Among the Hanafis, it is closely connected to their well-known theory regarding non-liability...
Source pages p. 973

Commentary
This passage introduces a maxim unique to the Hanafi school: 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an' (wage and liability do not combine). It defines 'ajr' as compensation for usufruct over a period, and 'daman' as the obligation to pay the value of the thing used. The author notes that this maxim is specifically Hanafi and not accepted by the majority of other schools. The Hanafi school connects it to their theory of non-liability (adam al-daman) in certain contexts, but the passage is cut off. This maxim is often applied in cases of rental or loan where the person who receives wages (rent) cannot also be held liable for the item's value if it is destroyed, as liability and wage are seen as mutually exclusive.

Key Terms
الأجر (al-ajr): Compensation or wage paid for the usufruct or benefit of a thing for a specific period.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability to pay the value of the thing used (the 'ayn) if it is destroyed or damaged.
الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان (al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an): The Hanafi maxim stating that wage and liability cannot combine on the same person simultaneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A person who rents an item is not liable for its value because he pays rent
C) The maxim applies to all schools of thought
D) The maxim combines wage and liability on the same person

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an' establishes that a hirer who pays rent is not liable for accidental destruction of the hired item, a rule specific to the Hanafi school.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

The Twenty-Fifth Maxim: Benefit and Burden Proportionality
Introduces the twenty-fifth maxim and equates it with two known principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the twenty-fifth maxim, explain its relationship to the maxims 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' and 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm', and apply the principle of proportional benefit and burden to a new case.

Core Idea
Benefit and burden are proportional: whoever enjoys the benefit must bear the risk, and whoever bears the burden is entitled to the benefit.

This lesson introduces the twenty-fifth legal maxim of the Hanafi school, which elegantly compresses a core principle of justice into two symmetrical statements. The maxim is not a standalone rule but a restatement of two complementary principles: profit accompanies liability, and liability is with gain. Understanding this maxim helps muftis ensure fairness in contracts and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Parse the maxim into its two halves
The maxim consists of two symmetrical clauses: 'al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah' (benefit is proportional to burden) and 'al-niqmah bi qadr al-ni'mah' (burden is proportional to benefit). Note the reversal.
2
Link each half to its synonymous maxim
The first half is synonymous with 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit accompanies liability). The second half is synonymous with 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (liability is with gain).
3
Understand the combined meaning
The maxim teaches that in any transaction, the party who enjoys a benefit must also bear the corresponding risk; conversely, the party who suffers a burden is entitled to the corresponding benefit.
4
Apply to a real case
Consider a sale where the seller delivers defective goods. The buyer's burden (defect) entitles him to a remedy (return or price reduction). This proportional relationship is derived from the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الخامسة والعشرون: النعمة بقدر النقمة، والنقمة بقدر النعمة» (م/ ۸۸). الجملة الأولى من هذه القاعدة ترادف قاعدة: الخراج بالضمان . والجملة الثانية منها ترادف القاعدة السابقة: «الغرم بالغنم».
The twenty-fifth maxim: 'Blessing is in proportion to affliction, and affliction is in proportion to blessing.' The first sentence of this maxim is synonymous with the maxim 'Profit accompanies liability' (al-kharaj bi al-daman). The second sentence is synonymous with the previous maxim 'Liability is with gain' (al-ghurm bi al-ghunm).
Source pages p. 973

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-fifth legal maxim, which consists of two symmetrical statements. The first part ('Blessing is in proportion to affliction') means that whoever enjoys a benefit must also bear the associated difficulties; this matches the earlier maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' (profit accompanies liability). The second part ('Affliction is in proportion to blessing') means that whoever suffers a burden is entitled to the corresponding benefit; this matches the twenty-fourth maxim 'al-ghurm bi al-ghunm' (liability is with gain). Thus, the maxim consolidates two complementary principles, emphasizing reciprocity between benefit and burden.

Key Terms
النعمة (al-ni'mah): Benefit, blessing, or profit derived from something.
النقمة (al-niqmah): Affliction, burden, or loss associated with something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which earlier maxim does the second sentence of the twenty-fifth maxim ('affliction is in proportion to blessing') correspond to?
A) al-kharaj bi al-daman
B) al-ghurm bi al-ghunm
C) al-ujr wa al-daman la yajtami'an
D) al-ni'mah bi qadr al-niqmah

Key Takeaway
The twenty-fifth maxim elegantly restates the twin principles of proportional benefit and burden, ensuring justice in legal transactions.

Al-Ujr wa al-Daman La Yajtami'an: Hanafi Maxim Analyzed
Explains a uniquely Hanafi maxim with applications and critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'al-ujr wa al-daman la yajtami'an', apply it to cases of hirer transgression, and evaluate the critique that potential liability is impractical.

Core Idea
Hanafi maxim: hire and liability cannot combine, even potential liability eliminates hire.

This lesson dissects the twenty-sixth Hanafi maxim: 'Hire and liability do not combine.' We will explore its definition, the Hanafi interpretation of 'daman' as potential liability, and its application to a hirer who becomes a usurper. The author presents and then critiques this view, offering the majority position as a stronger alternative.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its terms
Identify the definition of ujr (hire) and daman (liability) in this context. Note that Hanafis interpret daman as potential liability, not actual.
The maxim is wusta (intermediate) rank.
2
Analyze the Hanafi application
Work through the example: a hirer takes the animal to a wrong destination. Trace the change from amin to muta'addi (usurper). Apply the maxim to two outcomes: animal perishes (no hire) and animal returns safely (no hire either).
Hanafis say even potential liability suffices to cancel hire.
3
Critique the rationale
Identify the author's criticism: potential liability is a theoretical construct that opens doors to hiyal (legal stratagems) and lacks practical benefit.
Author suggests restricting to actual liability would be more defensible.
4
Compare with majority view
Contrast the Hanafi position with the majority (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) who allow hire and liability to coexist. The hirer would owe both hire for benefit and liability for destruction.
Thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السادسة والعشرون: الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان» (م/٨٦). الأجر بدل المنفعة عن مدة ما. والضمان المقصود هنا هو الالتزام بقيمة العين المنتفع بها (ر : ف (۸۰/۸۱) . وهذه القاعدة حنفية النسب لا يتبناها جمهور المذاهب الفقهية الأخرى. عند الحنفية تتصل اتصالاً وثيقاً بنظريتهم المشهورة في عدم ضمان الغاصب منافع المغصوب تلك النظرية التي تقدمت الإشارة إليها وإلى انتقادها (ر: ف ۳/۷۷). - وعلى هذه القاعدة يفرّع الحنفية أنه : لو استأجر شخص دابة مثلاً ليركبها إلى مكان معين، فذهب بها رأساً إلى مكان آخر يعتبر متعدياً في حكم الغاصب، ويخرج عن صفة الأمين التي هي الصفة الأصلية شرعاً للمستأجر، فإذا هلكت الدابة عنده قبل ردها إلى مالكها يضمن قيمتها ولا أجر عليه لأنَّ الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان . - وكذا إذا لم تهلك فردها سليمة ولو بعد شهور لا أجر عليه أيضاً !! لأنه كان في حالة مسؤولية بضمانها لو هلكت عنده (ر: أول بحث إجارة الدواب من الفتاوى الخانية وقواعد الحمزاوي مسائل الإجارة ص/ ۸۲). يتضح من هذه التفريعات وأمثالها أنه ليس المراد بالضمان في هذه القاعدة الضمان المتحقق الواقع فعلاً أي بأن تهلك العين فيلتزم الشخص بقيمتها، بل المراد به في النظر الحنفي كون الشخص عرضة لضمان العين، وذلك بأن يكون بحالة يعتبر فيها هو المسؤول بقيمة العين إن هلكت، سواء أوقع الهلاك فعلاً أم لم يقع، أي أن يكون في حالة تحمل التبعة. وهذا في منتهى الغرابة كما ترى فهو تفريح نظري محض لم ينظر فيه إلى مصلحة التطبيق. فهو يفسح للناس مجال الاحتيال للانتفاع بأموال الغير بلا بدل فيعقدون الإجارة على غير المنفعة التي يريدون، ثم يخالفون إلى الانتفاع بما يريدون دون التزام بعوض ولا يبالون ضمان المأجور عند الهلاك لأنه نادر. ولو أن فقهاء الحنفية خصصوا القاعدة بحالة وقوع الضمان وتحققه، كما إذا هلكت الدابة مثلاً في هذه الحال والتزم المستأجر بقيمتها لكان لها وجه، إذ يقال أن ضمان الأصل تندمج فيه المنافع مع أن ذلك أيضاً غير قوي. وجمهور المذاهب الأخرى يجتمع في نظرها الأجر والضمان. ففي هذه الحادثة مثلاً يلتزم المستأجر بأجر المثل عن المنافع التي استوفاها بلا
The twenty-sixth maxim: 'Hire and liability do not combine.' Hire is the compensation for usufruct over a period. Liability intended here is the obligation to pay the value of the thing used. This maxim is Hanafi in origin; it is not adopted by the majority of other jurisprudential schools. For Hanafis, it is closely connected to their famous theory of the usurper (ghasib) not being liable for the usufruct of the usurped object, a theory previously mentioned and criticized. - Based on this maxim, Hanafis derive that if a person hires an animal, for example, to ride to a specific place, but he goes straight to another place, he becomes a transgressor (muta'addi) in the ruling of a usurper, and leaves the status of trustee (amin) which is the original legal status of the hirer. If the animal perishes with him before returning it to its owner, he must guarantee its value and owes no hire because hire and liability do not combine. - Likewise, even if it does not perish and he returns it safely, even after months, he owes no hire either!! because he was in a state of liability (responsible for its value if it had perished). It becomes clear from these derived cases and their like that what is meant by 'daman' in this maxim is not actual, realized liability (i.e., the thing actually perishes and the person is obligated for its value), but rather in the Hanafi view it means the person being subject to potential liability for the thing: that he is in a condition where he is considered responsible for the value of the thing if it perishes, whether the destruction occurs or not, i.e., he is in a state of bearing the risk. This is extremely strange, as you see; it is a purely theoretical derivation that does not consider practical benefit. It opens the door for people to devise stratagems to benefit from others' property without compensation: they enter into a hire contract for a different usufruct than what they intend, then violate and use what they want without being bound by any consideration, and they do not care about liability for the hired item in case of destruction because that is rare. If the Hanafi jurists had restricted the maxim to the case of actual realized liability, as when the animal perishes and the hirer is obligated for its value, it would have some justification, though even that is not strong. The majority of other schools hold that hire and liability can coexist. In this incident, for example, the hirer is obligated for the equivalent hire for the usufruct he actually derived without...
Source pages p. 973

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-sixth maxim: 'Hire and liability do not combine' (al-ujr wa al-daman la yajtami'an). The author defines the key terms: 'ujr' is compensation for usufruct, and 'daman' here means responsibility for the value of the object. This maxim is distinctively Hanafi, not accepted by the majority of other schools. The Hanafi school bases it on their theory that a usurper (ghasib) is not liable for the usufruct of the usurped item. The author then gives a typical Hanafi application: if a person hires a mount to ride to a specific place but takes it elsewhere, he becomes a usurper and loses his trustee status. If the animal perishes, he must pay its value but not the hire, because hire and liability cannot combine. Remarkably, even if the animal survives and is returned safely, he still owes no hire, because he was in a state of potential liability throughout. The author criticizes this interpretation, noting that it defines 'daman' as potential liability (being subject to risk) rather than actual liability. This, he argues, is a purely theoretical construct that overlooks practical utility and opens the door for legal stratagery (hiyal) to use others' property without paying. The author suggests that restricting the maxim to cases of actual liability would be more defensible. He also notes that other schools allow the combination of hire and liability; for instance, a hirer who misuses the item must pay both the hire for the benefit received and the value if it perishes.

Key Terms
الأجر (al-ujr): Compensation (hire) for the benefit or usufruct of an item over a specified period.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability; in this maxim it means being subject to potential liability (i.e., responsible for the value if the item is destroyed).
المستأجر (al-musta'jir): The hirer; in the example, the person who rents the animal.
الأمين (al-amin): Trustee; the original legal status of a hirer (musta'jir) unless he transgresses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'al-ujr wa al-daman la yajtami'an', if a hirer misuses a hired animal and returns it safely after months, what is the ruling on hire?
A) He must pay the agreed hire
B) He must pay the market hire
C) He owes no hire because he was in a state of potential liability
D) He owes half the hire

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'al-ujr wa al-daman la yajtami'an' denies hire when there is potential liability, but this interpretation is criticized as impractical and enabling stratagems; the majority allows both.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims
intermediate
6 min
Private Authority Stronger than Public Authority
Explaining maxim #33 with exceptions for public interest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-wilayah al-khassah aqwa min al-wilayah al-ammah' to determine when a judge may override a private trustee or guardian.

Core Idea
Private authority trumps public authority, except when public interest demands otherwise.

In Hanafi fiqh, the relationship between private trustees (such as guardians for minors or administrators of waqf) and the public authority of the judge is governed by a clear maxim: private authority is stronger. This lesson unpacks that maxim, its applications, and its critical exceptions. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for any mufti dealing with property disputes or waqf management.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Begin by memorizing the maxim in Arabic: الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة. Understand that it applies broadly to all chapters where private and public authority conflict, especially in guardianship and waqf.
The maxim is number 59 in the Majalla.
2
Identify the furuat (applications)
The passage gives two direct applications: the judge cannot dispose of a minor's property if a guardian exists, and cannot dispose of waqf property if a trustee exists. Identify the ruling in each case.
These are the default rules; private authority prevails.
3
Analyze the istithna'at (exceptions)
Note the two exceptions: the judge may lease waqf property for long-term renovation, and may lend the minor's property when the guardian fails. Understand that these exceptions are driven by public interest.
Exceptions are limited to cases where private authority fails to protect the property.
4
Understand the talil (reason) for the exceptions
The reason is that safeguarding waqf and orphan property is a public right. The judge, as the public guardian, can step in to preserve the property, and can even audit or remove negligent trustees.
This connects to the complementary maxim: 'التصرف على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة' (Disposition over subjects is tied to public interest).
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a modern case: a waqf building is in disrepair, the mutawalli refuses to lease it for renovation. The judge may authorize the lease. Apply the maxim and its exception to reach the ruling.
This step trains the student to operationalize the maxim in fatwa-giving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۳/۱ - القاعدة الثالثة والثلاثون: الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة» (م/٥٩).
فلا يملك القاضي أن يتصرف في مال القاصر أو الوقف مع وجود الوصي أو المتولي.
وقد تقدم الكلام على هذه القاعدة في نظرية الأهلية والولاية (ر: ف .(٩/٦٦
الاستثناءات :
. ويستثنى من القاعدة أن للقاضي حق إيجار عقار الوقف مدة طويلة عند الحاجة إلى التعمير وليس للمتولي ذلك.
- وكذا للقاضي إقراض مال القاصر، وليس للأب أو للوصي
ر ف ٦/٦٢).
ذلك
والسبب في هذا الاستثناء أن صيانة مال الأوقاف والأيتام هي من الحق العام. فتجاوز الحدود المسموحة للأولياء والأوصياء والقوام يعود إلى تقدير الوليّ العام. ولذا كان للقاضي محاسبتهم وعزلهم عند الاقتضاء
بمقتضى النظر العام (الولاية العامة وإن كان ليس له أن يباشر العقود عنهم مع وجودهم (من) شرح والدي رحمه الله على القواعد).
93/1 - The thirty-third maxim: Private authority is stronger than public authority. So the judge cannot dispose of the property of a minor or a waqf while a guardian or trustee exists. Discussion of this maxim has preceded in the theory of capacity and authority. Exceptions: An exception from the maxim is that the judge has the right to lease a waqf property for a long period when there is need for renovation, and the trustee does not have that. Similarly, the judge has the right to lend the property of a minor, and the father or guardian does not have that. The reason for this exception is that safeguarding the property of waqfs and orphans is a public right. So exceeding the permissible limits for guardians, executors, and trustees reverts to the estimation of the general guardian. Hence the judge has the right to audit them and dismiss them when necessary by virtue of general supervision (public authority, even though he cannot directly contract on their behalf while they exist, from my father's commentary on the maxims).
Source pages p. 984

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim from the Majalla, number 59, regarding the relative strength of private versus public authority. The maxim establishes that private authority (e.g., a guardian over a minor or a trustee over a waqf) takes precedence over public authority (the judge). The judge cannot intervene in the management of their property while they are present and capable. However, exceptions are made when the public interest demands: the judge may lease waqf property for long-term renovation if the trustee cannot, and may lend the minor's property if the guardian fails to do so. The reason is that preserving waqf and orphan property is a public right, and the general guardian (the ruler or judge) has ultimate oversight to ensure proper management, including the power to audit and remove negligent trustees.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilāyah al-khāṣṣah): Authority granted to a specific individual over a particular matter, e.g., a guardian over a minor or a trustee over a waqf.
الولاية العامة (al-wilāyah al-ʿāmmah): Authority vested in the ruler or judge over general affairs of the community.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The guardian appointed by the father or the court for a minor.
المتولي (al-mutawallī): The administrator or trustee of a waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'Private authority is stronger than public authority,' in which situation can the judge override the private trustee?
A) Whenever the judge deems it beneficial
B) When the trustee is present and capable
C) When there is need to lease waqf property for renovation
D) The judge can never override

Key Takeaway
Private authority prevails unless public interest necessitates intervention, limited to specific exceptions like renovation of waqf or lending of a minor's property.

Maxim 58: Action Concerning Subjects is Contingent upon Public Interest
Explaining maxim #58 on limits of ruler's authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-tasarruf ala al-ra'iyyah manut bi al-maslahah' to identify permissible and impermissible government actions in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
All governmental actions must serve public interest; any deviation is impermissible.

This lesson explores a foundational maxim that governs the relationship between rulers and subjects in Islamic law. You will learn how this maxim restricts governmental authority and ensures accountability, drawing on examples from hadd punishments, judicial rulings, and public funds.

Guided Path
1
Articulate the maxim
State the maxim in Arabic and English and explain its core meaning: every authoritative action over subjects is dependent on achieving public interest.
The phrase 'public interest' (maslahah) here refers to what secures the community's welfare within Sharia.
2
Identify the authority basis
Understand why rulers are not owners of authority but agents of the community (wukala' al-ummah). Their legitimacy is conditional on serving the public good.
This foundational view prevents tyranny (istibdad) and self-aggrandizement (istiththar).
3
Trace the applications (furuat)
List the four specific prohibitions mentioned in the text and connect each to the maxim.
Each prohibition is derived from the maxim and illustrates its scope.
4
Connect each furu' to the maxim
For each prohibition, explain why it serves public interest: e.g., pardoning hadd encourages crime, harming public safety.
This reasoning (ta'lil) is essential for applying the maxim to new cases.
5
Recognize limits and complementary maxims
Note that the maxim has no exceptions in the furuat given, but it is complemented by other maxims such as 'private guardianship is stronger than public guardianship'.
Understanding complementarity prevents overextension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۴/۱ - القاعدة الرابعة والثلاثون: التصرُّف على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة (م/٥٨).
هذه القاعدة ترسم حدود الإدارة العامة والسياسة الشرعية في سلطان الولاة وتصرفاتهم على الرعية. فتفيد أن أعمال هؤلاء الولاة وتصرفاتهم النافذة على الرعية الملزمة لها في حقوقها العامة والخاصة يجب أن تبنى على مصلحة الجماعة وتهدف إلى خيرها.
ذلك لأنَّ الولاة من الخليفة فمن دونه من العمال الموظفين في الأمة فروع السلطة الحكومية، ليسوا عمالاً لأنفسهم، وإنما هم وكلاء عن الأمة في القيام بأصلح التدابير لإقامة العدل، ودفع الظلم، وصيانة الحقوق والأخلاق، وضبط الأمن، ونشر العلم وتسهيل المرافق العامة، وتطهير المجتمع من الفساد وتحقيق كل ما هو خير للأمة في حاضرها ومستقبلها بأفضل الوسائل، مما يعبر عنه بالمصلحة العامة.
فكل عمل أو تصرف من الولاة على خلاف هذه المصلحة، مما يقصد به استثثار أو استبداد أو يؤدّي إلى ضرر أو فساد هو غير جائز: فليس لولي الأمر أن يعفو عن عقوبات الحدود مطلقاً، ولا عن غيرها من الجرائم أو العقوبات إذا كان في ذلك تشجيع على الإجرام واستخفاف بنتائجه.
وكذا ليس له أن يهدر الحقوق الشخصية للمجنى عليهم بحال من الأحوال. ولا أن يبطل أقضية القضاة
- وكذا ليس لإمام أو أمير أو قاض أن يمنع محاسبة من تحت أيديهم أموال العامة أو القاصرين كالمتولين على الأوقاف وكالأوصياء.
94/1 - The thirty-fourth maxim: Action concerning subjects is contingent upon public interest. This maxim outlines the boundaries of public administration and Islamic policy in the authority of governors and their actions over subjects. It indicates that the actions of these governors and their enforceable decisions over subjects, binding on them in their public and private rights, must be based on the community's interest and aimed at its good. That is because the governors from the caliph down to the employed officials in the nation are branches of governmental authority; they are not agents for themselves, but rather representatives of the nation in taking the best measures to establish justice, repel oppression, protect rights and morals, maintain security, spread knowledge, facilitate public facilities, purify society from corruption, and achieve all that is good for the nation in its present and future with the best means—which is called the public interest. So every action or measure of governors contrary to this interest, intended for self-aggrandizement or despotism, or leading to harm or corruption, is impermissible: the ruler may not pardon hadd punishments absolutely, nor other crimes or punishments if that encourages crime and belittles its consequences. Similarly, he may not disregard the personal rights of victims under any circumstances, nor invalidate the judgments of judges. Likewise, an imam, amir, or judge may not prevent the auditing of those under their authority regarding public funds or those of minors, such as trustees of waqfs and guardians.
Source pages p. 985

Commentary
This passage presents the thirty-fourth maxim from the Majalla, number 58, which is a foundational principle for Islamic governance. The maxim states that all governmental actions affecting the subjects must be grounded in public interest. The text explains that rulers are not owners of authority but agents of the community, tasked with justice, security, education, and public welfare. Any action contrary to this—whether motivated by personal gain, tyranny, or leading to harm—is prohibited. Specific prohibitions include: the ruler cannot pardon hadd punishments (as they are rights of God and the community), cannot waive other punishments if it encourages crime, cannot ignore victims' personal rights, cannot overturn valid court judgments, and cannot prevent accountability of those managing public or orphan funds. This maxim imposes strict limits on governmental power and emphasizes accountability.

Key Terms
الرعية (al-raʿiyyah): The subjects or citizens under the ruler's authority.
المصلحة (al-maṣlaḥah): Public interest or welfare that guides all governmental actions.
الاستثثار (al-istiththār): Self-aggrandizement or seeking personal benefit at the expense of public interest.
الاستبداد (al-istibdād): Despotism or arbitrary rule without regard for law or consultation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, why is a ruler not allowed to pardon hadd punishments?
A) Because hadd punishments are rights of God and the community
B) Because the ruler lacks authority over such matters
C) Because the victim must consent first
D) Because hadd punishments are not mentioned in the maxim

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'Action concerning subjects is contingent upon public interest' establishes that all governmental authority is fiduciary and must be exercised for the community's welfare, with explicit limits prohibiting specific abuses.

6 min
Ruler's Authority is Conditional on Public Interest
Explains the maxim and its applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'التصرف على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة' to evaluate whether a ruler's action serves public interest and state the corresponding ruling (valid/impermissible).

Core Idea
A ruler's authority is a trust, not a right; every action must serve the public good.

This lesson unpacks one of the major comprehensive maxims of Hanafi fiqh: 'Disposition over the subjects is conditional upon benefit.' It distinguishes legitimate governance from arbitrary rule, grounding all public administration in the concept of maslahah (public interest). The passage provides concrete examples and prophetic hadiths that define the limits of authority. By mastering this maxim, you will be able to assess governmental actions from a shar'i perspective.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Identify the maxim in Arabic and English. Note that it is an intermediate (wusta) comprehensive maxim governing public administration and siyasah shariyyah.
The full Arabic: التصرُّف على الرعية منوط بالمصلحة
2
List the five furuat (applications)
From the text, extract the five specific rulings derived from the maxim. Each is a prohibition or obligation on the ruler.
Examples include no pardoning hudud if it encourages crime, no misuse of public funds, no appointing unqualified persons.
3
Analyze the hadith proofs
The author cites several ahadith to support the maxim. Identify the key phrases that establish the duty of the ruler to be sincere and just, and the prohibition against deceit.
Focus on 'غاش رعيته' (deceiving subjects) and 'لا طاعة لمخلوق في معصية الله'.
4
Examine the istithna' (exception)
Note that the maxim allows flexibility in ta'zir (discretionary) punishments if public interest requires leniency within shar'i bounds.
This is not a true exception but an application of the same maxim.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Using the maxim, analyze a contemporary issue such as a government imposing a tax on a haram activity to raise revenue.
The maxim prohibits such a policy because it promotes evil.

Chapter Ten: The Comprehensive Maxims in Fiqh. By virtue of general consideration (general guardianship, although he does not have the right to conduct contracts on their behalf in their presence) from the commentary of my father (may Allah have mercy on him) on the Qawa'id. 94/1 - The Thirty-Fourth Maxim: The disposition over the subjects is conditional upon benefit (M/58). This maxim delineates the boundaries of public administration and shar'i policy in the authority of governors and their actions over the subjects. It indicates that the works of these governors and their binding dispositions over the subjects in their public and private rights must be built on the benefit of the community and aim at its good. That is because the governors, from the Caliph down to the workers employed in the nation, are branches of governmental authority; they are not workers for themselves, but rather agents for the nation in undertaking the best measures to establish justice, repel oppression, protect rights and morals, maintain security, spread knowledge, facilitate public utilities, purify society from corruption, and achieve everything that is good for the nation in its present and future by the best means, which is expressed as the public interest. So every act or disposition of governors contrary to this benefit, which is intended for exploitation or tyranny or leads to harm or corruption, is impermissible: the ruler is not allowed to pardon hudud punishments absolutely, nor other crimes or punishments if that encourages crime and makes light of its consequences. Likewise, he is not allowed to nullify the personal rights of the victims under any circumstances. Nor to invalidate the judgments of judges. - Likewise, an Imam, prince, or judge is not allowed to prevent the accountability of those under their authority regarding public funds or funds of minors, such as trustees of endowments and guardians. - Nor to permit anything from prohibited evils like immorality, alcohol, and gambling, even under the pretext of collecting wealth and taxes from them. - Nor to appoint someone dishonest or incompetent. The basis for that is the statement of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Any servant whom Allah places in charge of subjects and dies while deceiving his subjects, Allah will forbid Paradise to him.' Reported by Bukhari in al-Ahkam and Muslim in al-Iman. And also his statement: 'There is no Amir who is put in charge of the affairs of Muslims, then does not strive for them and advise them as he advises and strives for himself, except that he will not enter Paradise with them.' Reported by Muslim and al-Tabarani, and the phrase 'as he advises and strives for himself' is in al-Tabarani's narration. And his statement: 'Whoever appoints a man from a group (i.e., a community) while there is among them one who is more pleasing to Allah than him, has betrayed Allah, His Messenger, and the believers.' Reported by al-Hakim in al-Ahkam from Ibn Abbas. And his statement: 'There is no obedience to a created being in disobedience to Allah.' Reported by Ahmad in al-Musnad from the hadith of Imran and Hakam ibn Amr al-Ghifari. (M/71)
Source pages p. 985

Commentary
This passage expounds the Thirty-Fourth Comprehensive Maxim: 'Disposition over the subjects is conditional upon benefit.' It is a ruling on the method of issuing fatwas concerning the limits of public administration. The author explains that all actions of rulers and officials must be based on public interest. They are agents of the community, not rulers for themselves. The maxim prohibits actions contrary to public interest, such as pardoning hudud to encourage crime, violating personal rights, preventing accountability of public funds, permitting haram activities for tax revenue, and appointing unqualified persons. The author supports the maxim with several hadiths emphasizing the duty of rulers to be sincere and just, and the prohibition of obeying them in sin.

Key Terms
المصلحة (al-maslahah): Public interest that includes justice, security, and all good for the community in the present and future.
الرعية (al-ra'iyyah): The subjects under the ruler's authority, including Muslims and dhimmis in public rights.
الولاة (al-wulah): The rulers, from the caliph down to government employees.
