Rule 36: Evidence of a Thing in Internal Matters Takes Its Place
Explains the 36th maxim of the Majalla on circumstantial evidence in hidden matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the 36th maxim of the Majalla to determine when external evidence substitutes for hidden internal matters in Hanafi fiqh, and identify exceptions when the hidden reality is definitively known.

Core Idea
In hidden matters, external evidence legally substitutes for the reality, unless certainty overrides.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Hanafi legal proof: when internal states like intention or truthfulness are inaccessible, Shariah relies on external indicators. The maxim 'Evidence of a thing in internal matters takes its place' (Majalla 68) governs this substitution. You'll learn how it applies to contracts, homicide, and claims, and when exceptions apply based on certainty.

Guided Path
1
Comprehend the maxim and its scope
Read the maxim carefully: 'دليل الشيء في الأمور الباطنة يقوم مقامه'. Internal matters (al-umur al-batina) are hidden realities that determine rulings. The maxim states that evidence of such matters legally occupies their place, so rulings attach to the evidence, not the hidden reality.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, governing all fiqh chapters where internal states matter.
2
Analyze the first application: contracts
Real intention (niyyah) in contracts is hidden. The apparent intention through offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) is taken as evidence. The contract is valid based on this evidence, unless the lack of real intention is proven (e.g., duress or jest).
This aligns with the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' but with external evidence as proxy.
3
Analyze the second application: homicide
If a killer uses a deadly weapon, that act is evidence of intent to kill. A claim of mistake is not accepted because the weapon's use substitutes for the hidden intention. The ruling attaches to the evidence, not the claimed hidden state.
This is a rebuttable presumption? No, the passage says claim of mistake is not accepted; evidence is conclusive unless contrary is proven (e.g., the weapon was used accidentally in self-defense, but that would need proof).
4
Analyze the third application: prescription of claims
Abandoning a claim for a long period without valid excuse is evidence that the claimant is not truthful. The claim is rejected based on this evidence, unless the opponent admits the right, removing doubt.
This relates to the principle that delay in claiming without excuse indicates lack of truthfulness.
5
Identify the exception: certainty overrides
The maxim applies only as long as the hidden reality is unknown. If the internal reality becomes definitively known (e.g., proven lack of intent in a contract via evidence), the evidence no longer stands. This is governed by the maxim 'al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk'.
This exception prevents the maxim from overriding certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩٦/٨١ - القاعدة السادسة والثلاثون: دليل الشيء في الأمور الباطنة يقوم مقامه» (م/ ٦٨).
إنَّ لهذه القاعدة صلة وثيقة بمبدأ القضاء بالقرائن .
الأمور الباطنة هي الحقائق الخفية المحجوبة التي يتعذر أو يتعشر الاطلاع عليها مع أنها تختلف بوجودها وعدمها الأحكام، وتحتاج إلى إثبات .
فالنظر الشرعي أن ما كان كذلك لا يبحث عن حقيقة وجوده في الواقع، بل ينظر إلى دلائله، فيقوم وجودها مقام وجوده، وترتبط الأحكام بتلك الدلائل وجوداً وعدماً ولا يلتفت إلى احتمال خلافها :
- فالإرادة الحقيقية مثلاً في العقود هي خفاء، فيقوم دليلها وهو الإرادة الظاهرة بالإيجاب والقبول ،مقامها فينعقد بهما العقد ما لم يتحقق انتفاء الإرادة الحقيقية كما تقدم (ر: ف ٤/٣٢).
- واستعمال القاتل آلة قتالة دليل على قصده القتل، فلا يقبل منه ادعاء الخطأ، بل يقوم وجود الآلة القتالة مقام القصد الباطني الخفي.
أنَّ ترك الادعاء بالحق مدة التقادم مع عدم المانع من الادعاء دليل المدعي غير محق في الواقع فلا تقبل دعواه إلا أن يقر خصمه فتنتفي الريبة ( ر : ف (٤/٢٣ .
96/81 - The thirty-sixth maxim: 'The evidence of a thing in internal matters takes its place.' (M/68)
This maxim is closely related to the principle of judging by circumstantial evidence.
Internal matters are hidden and concealed realities that are difficult or hard to ascertain, yet rulings differ based on their presence or absence, and they need proof.
The Shariah perspective is that such matters are not investigated as to their actual existence in reality, but rather their evidences are looked at; their existence stands in place of its existence, and rulings are attached to those evidences in terms of existence and non-existence, and no attention is paid to the possibility of contrary.
- For example, real intention in contracts is hidden, so its evidence—the apparent intention through offer and acceptance—takes its place, and the contract is concluded by them as long as the non-existence of real intention is not verified, as previously mentioned (ref: section 4/32).
- And the killer's use of a deadly weapon is evidence of his intent to kill, so a claim of mistake is not accepted from him; rather, the existence of the deadly weapon stands in place of the hidden inner intent.
- And the abandonment of claiming a right for the period of prescription without a hindrance to claiming is evidence that the claimant is not truly entitled, so his claim is not accepted unless his opponent admits, thus removing doubt (ref: section 4/23).
Source pages p. 987

Commentary
This passage presents the thirty-sixth maxim from the Majalla, which deals with circumstantial evidence. The author explains that internal matters (al-umur al-batina) are hidden realities that affect legal rulings but are difficult to ascertain directly. Shariah does not require investigating the actual hidden truth; instead, it relies on external evidence that indicates that truth. The evidence legally stands in place of the hidden matter, and rulings are based on that evidence regardless of any contrary possibility. Three examples are given: (1) In contracts, the real intention is hidden, but the apparent intention expressed through offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) is taken as evidence, and the contract is valid unless actual lack of intention is confirmed. (2) If a killer uses a deadly weapon, that is evidence of intent to kill; a claim of accident is not accepted because the weapon's use substitutes for the hidden intention. (3) If a claimant abandons his claim for a long period (prescription) without a legal excuse, that is evidence that he is not in the right, so his claim is rejected unless the opponent admits it, thereby removing suspicion. This maxim underpins the principle that Shariah relies on apparent indicators in internal matters.

Key Terms
الأمور الباطنة (al-umur al-batina): Hidden realities that cannot be directly observed, such as intention, consent, or truthfulness.
القرائن (al-qara'in): Circumstantial evidence or indicators that lead to knowledge of hidden matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the 36th maxim, what is the legal effect of a killer using a deadly weapon?
A) It proves the killer is guilty of murder
B) It is evidence of intent to kill, and a claim of mistake is not accepted
C) It creates a presumption of killing but allows the killer to prove accident
D) It has no legal significance without witnesses

Key Takeaway
External evidence legally substitutes for hidden internal realities unless the hidden reality becomes certain.

The Thirty-Sixth Maxim: Evidence Stands in Place of the Hidden
Explains a major legal maxim with applications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the thirty-sixth major legal maxim, identify its scope, and apply it to the furuat (applications) provided in the text.

Core Idea
When direct knowledge is impossible, the law treats reliable evidence as if it were the hidden reality itself.

This passage presents one of the major legal maxims codified in the Majalla: 'The evidence of a thing in internal matters stands in its place.' Mustafa al-Zarqa explains the legal logic behind this maxim, linking it to the principle of judging by circumstantial evidence (qada' bil-qara'in). The maxim allows judges and muftis to base rulings on reliable external evidence when the inner reality is inaccessible, which is a cornerstone of Hanafi legal reasoning. The text then gives three classic applications in contracts, criminal law, and procedural law, plus a modern-sounding application in public administration.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim verbatim
Write the maxim in Arabic and English: 'دليل الشيء في الأمور الباطنة يقوم مقامه' / 'The evidence of a thing in internal matters stands in its place.' Note that it is a wusta (intermediate) and kulliya (universal) maxim.
Memorize the exact wording; it is canonical.
2
Understand the legal philosophy
Islamic law does not require proving hidden realities directly; their evidence suffices. The opposite possibility is ignored unless certainty to the contrary exists.
This links to the principle of qada' bil-qara'in (judging by circumstantial evidence).
3
Analyze the furuat (applications)
For each application, identify which hidden reality is involved, what evidence stands in its place, and the resulting ruling. For example, in contracts, actual intent is hidden; the outward expression (offer and acceptance) is the evidence and the contract is concluded.
The author gives three main furuat plus a fourth from al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
4
Identify the istithna' (exception)
When the hidden reality is definitely known (e.g., actual intent is clearly absent despite outward expression), the evidence is superseded by certainty. The competing qawida is 'اليقين لا يزول بالشك' (Certainty is not removed by doubt).
This prevents abuse of the maxim.
5
Link to the mufti's toolkit
This maxim is a tool for handling cases where direct proof is impossible. The mufti must ensure the evidence is reliable and consider the adab of not applying it when certainty exists.
It is a fundamental principle in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
القاعدة السادسة والثلاثون: دليل الشيء في الأمور الباطنة يقوم مقامه» (م/ ٦٨).
إنَّ لهذه القاعدة صلة وثيقة بمبدأ القضاء بالقرائن .
الأمور الباطنة هي الحقائق الخفية المحجوبة التي يتعذر أو يتعشر الاطلاع عليها مع أنها تختلف بوجودها وعدمها الأحكام، وتحتاج إلى إثبات .
فالنظر الشرعي أن ما كان كذلك لا يبحث عن حقيقة وجوده في الواقع، بل ينظر إلى دلائله، فيقوم وجودها مقام وجوده، وترتبط الأحكام بتلك الدلائل وجوداً وعدماً ولا يلتفت إلى احتمال خلافها :
- فالإرادة الحقيقية مثلاً في العقود هي خفاء، فيقوم دليلها وهو الإرادة الظاهرة بالإيجاب والقبول ،مقامها فينعقد بهما العقد ما لم يتحقق انتفاء الإرادة الحقيقية كما تقدم (ر: ف ٤/٣٢).

ومن الفروع المهمة لهذه القاعدة في أعمال الإدارة العامة أن عمال الحكومة وجباة بيت المال ومتولي الأوقاف وكتبتها ونحوهم إذا ظهرت عليهم مظاهر الغنى وبنوا الأبنية دون أن يعرف لثرائهم مصدر، كان ذلك دليلاً على خيانتهم وارتشائهم. فيجوز عزلهم ومصادرة أموالهم، مالم يثبتوا لها مصدراً من شرح والدي رحمه الله على القواعد نقلاً عن «الدر المختار» قبيل بحث كفالة الرجلين من كتاب الكفالة) (۱) .
Chapter Ten: Major Legal Maxims. The Thirty-Sixth Maxim: "The evidence of a thing in internal matters stands in its place" (M/68). This maxim is closely related to the principle of judging by circumstantial evidence. Internal matters are hidden realities that are inaccessible or difficult to ascertain, yet rulings differ based on their existence or non-existence, and they need proof. The Islamic legal view is that such matters are not investigated for their actual existence in reality; rather, one looks at their evidence, and the existence of that evidence stands in place of the actual existence. Rulings are attached to that evidence in terms of existence and non-existence, and the possibility of the contrary is not considered. For example: Actual intent in contracts is hidden, so its evidence—the apparent intent expressed by offer and acceptance—stands in its place, and the contract is concluded by them as long as the absence of actual intent is not confirmed, as previously mentioned. Likewise, a killer's use of a lethal weapon is evidence of his intent to kill; his claim of mistake is not accepted; the existence of the lethal weapon stands in place of the hidden internal intent. Also, abandoning a claim to a right for the period of limitation, without any impediment to claiming, is evidence that the claimant is not actually entitled; thus his claim is not accepted unless the opponent acknowledges it, thereby removing suspicion. Important applications of this maxim in public administration: government employees, tax collectors, trustees of endowments, their scribes, and the like—if they display signs of wealth and build buildings without a known source for their riches, that is evidence of their betrayal and bribery. It is permissible to dismiss them and confiscate their wealth, unless they prove a source for it, as per my father's commentary on the maxims, quoting from al-Durr al-Mukhtar before the discussion on surety of two men from the book of Kafala.
Source pages p. 987

Commentary
This passage explains the Thirty-Sixth Major Maxim from the Majalla: "The evidence of a thing in internal matters stands in its place." The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by linking the maxim to the principle of judging by circumstantial evidence (qada' bil-qara'in). Internal matters are those hidden realities that are difficult to ascertain directly, yet legal rulings depend on them. Shari'ah does not require investigating the actual hidden reality; rather, it relies on its outward evidence. The existence of that evidence effectively replaces the hidden reality for legal purposes, and the contrary possibility is ignored. The author then gives three applications: (1) In contracts, actual intent is hidden, but the external expression (offer and acceptance) is taken as evidence of intent, and the contract is valid unless the absence of intent is proven. (2) In homicide, using a lethal weapon is evidence of intent to kill; the killer's claim of accident is not accepted. (3) If a claimant abandons his claim for a long period without valid excuse, that is evidence that his claim is false; his lawsuit is not accepted unless the opponent confesses, which removes suspicion. Finally, a very practical application: public officials who suddenly appear wealthy without known sources of income are presumed to have obtained it through betrayal or bribery. This is evidence for their dismissal and confiscation of wealth, unless they can prove a legitimate source. This is a form of circumstantial evidence allowed in Hanafi fiqh, though it must be applied with caution. The reference to al-Durr al-Mukhtar shows the Hanafi pedigree of this ruling.

Key Terms
الأمور الباطنة (al-umur al-batina): Hidden realities that are difficult to ascertain directly, but on which legal rulings depend.
دليل الشيء (dalil al-shay'): The external sign or evidence that indicates the existence of a hidden matter, which the law treats as sufficient proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is presumed innocent until proven guilty.
B) It is evidence of betrayal and bribery, allowing dismissal and confiscation unless he proves a source.
C) He must only be questioned, but no action can be taken.
D) His wealth is considered a gift and is immune from confiscation.

Key Takeaway
The thirty-sixth maxim allows muftis and judges to rely on reliable external evidence when inner realities are hidden, provided no certainty contradicts it.

Maxim: A Person is Held by His Confession
Explains the maxim with historical and modern applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrarihi' to identify when a person's statement or conduct constitutes a binding confession, and evaluate its application in modern 'unlawful gains' legislation.

Core Idea
Your own word can be the strongest evidence against you—the maxim binds a person by his voluntary confession.

This lesson explores the Thirty-Seventh Maxim of the Majalla: 'A person is held by his confession.' While seemingly simple, this maxim has profound implications in evidence law and governance. We will examine its classical application by Caliph 'Umar in cases of unexplained wealth and connect it to modern anti-corruption laws. Understanding this maxim helps a mufti evaluate when a person's statement or actions are legally binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core maxim
State the maxim in Arabic and English. Recognize that it is a universal (kulliya) maxim from the Majalla.
Memorize the Arabic: المرء مُؤَاخَذْ بإقراره.
2
Analyze the classical furu'
Examine the practice of Caliph 'Umar: he inventoried employees' wealth and confiscated unexplained excess. This treats unexplained wealth as an implied confession of misappropriation.
The maxim is applied here indirectly—possession of unexplained wealth is akin to a confession.
3
Extract the underlying principle
The maxim rests on the idea that a person's own admission (iqrar) is binding. In 'Umar's case, the employee's failure to account for excess wealth served as an admission of wrongdoing.
This is an expansion of the core maxim to circumstantial evidence.
4
Map to modern context
Connect to modern 'unlawful gains' laws that require asset declarations. The failure to explain wealth is treated as an implied confession, applying the same principle.
Hanafi fiqh provides the foundation for these legal remedies.
5
Note the exception
Confession made under duress or coercion is not binding, as per the hadith 'Raf'a 'an ummati al-khata' wa al-nisyan wa ma istukrihu 'alayh'. This is governed by the maxim 'al-darar yuzal'.
Always check voluntariness before applying the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة السابعة والثلاثون: المرء مُؤَاخَذْ بإقراره» (م/۷۹).
(۱) وقد فعل ذلك عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه، فكان إذا استعمل عاملاً (موظفاً) أحصى ماله في سجل مكتوب. ثم إذا وجد عنده فضلاً ليس له مصدر صادره أو شاطره إياه على حسب قوة التهمة ووضعه في بيت المال. وقد مر عمر ببناء يبني بالحجارة والجص، فقال: لمن هذا؟ فذكروا عاملاً له على البحرين فقال: أبت الدراهم إلا أن تخرج أعناقها وشاطره ماله .
وكان يقول : لي على كل خائن أمينان: الماء والطين:

ما قلاص وأعبد بعتها بمائة ديناره؟ [القلاص (بكسر القاف) جمع «قلوص» (بفتح القاف)، وهي الناقة الفتية ] .
قال : خرجت بنفقة لي فاتجرت فيها . قال: إنا والله ما بعثناك للتجارة، أذها». 
قال : أما والله لا أعمل لك بعدها.
فقال عمر: أنا والله لا أستعملك بعدها !!
(ر) سيرة عمر بن الخطاب للأستاذين القاضيين الأخوين علي وناجي الطنطاوي ١/ ۲۳۱ - ۲۳۳ ، والإصابة ٤/ ۲۲۰ ، وعيون الأخبار لابن قتيبة ٥٣/١). وهذا ما يعبر عنه المعاصرون اليوم بقاعدة: من أين لك هذا»؟ ويطالبون بسن قوانين تسوغه، بعد أن أصبح كثيرون من رجال الحكم والموظفين على غنى واسع جديد لا يُعرف له مصدر عندهم سوى الخيانة واستغلال الوظيفة .
وقد كان في هذه المبادىء والأحكام الشرعية العلاج التشريعي المطلوب. وقد صدر لدينا فعلاً بعد الوحدة بين مصر وسورية قانون الكسب غير المشروع» سنة ١٩٥٨م أوجب على كل موظف ومن سيتوظف تقديم بيان يحصي فيه كل ما لديه من أموال له أو لزوجته أو لأولاده، ولكن طبق فترة ثم أهمل.
The Thirty-Seventh Maxim: "A person is held by his confession" (M/79). (1) And 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (may Allah be pleased with him) did that: whenever he appointed an employee, he would inventory his wealth in a written register. Then if he found an excess with no source, he confiscated it or divided it with him according to the strength of suspicion, and placed it in the public treasury. 'Umar once passed by a building being built with stones and plaster, and asked: "Whose is this?" They mentioned an employee of his in Bahrain. He said: "Dirhams refuse but to show their necks" and he divided his wealth. He used to say: "For every traitor I have two trustees: water and clay." He confiscated the wealth of al-Harith ibn Wahb al-Laythi and said to him: "What about the young she-camels and slaves you sold for a hundred dinars?" [Al-qilas (with kasr of qaf) is plural of qalus (with fatha of qaf) which is the young she-camel.] He said: "I went out with my expenses and traded with them." He said: "By Allah, we did not send you to trade; take it." He said: "By Allah, I will not work for you after this." 'Umar said: "By Allah, I will not appoint you after this!" (Ref: Sirat 'Umar by Ali and Naji al-Tantawi 1/231-233, al-Isaba 4/220, 'Uyun al-Akhbar by Ibn Qutayba 1/53). This is what contemporaries today express as the principle: "From where did you get this?" And they demand enactment of laws that permit it, after many rulers and employees have acquired vast new wealth for which they have no known source except betrayal and exploitation of position. These Islamic principles and rulings contained the required legislative remedy. Actually, after the union between Egypt and Syria, the "Unlawful Gains" law was issued in 1958, requiring every employee and future employee to submit a statement inventorying all assets belonging to him, his wife, or his children, but it was applied for a time and then neglected.
Source pages p. 988

Commentary
This passage presents the Thirty-Seventh Maxim from the Majalla: "A person is held by his confession." The maxim is straightforward: one's own admission is binding upon him. The author then illustrates this with the practice of Caliph 'Umar ibn al-Khattab (RA). 'Umar would record an employee's wealth before appointment, and if later there was unexplained surplus, he would confiscate it or take half. This is not a confession in the strict sense, but rather the principle that a person's actions and circumstances can be taken as indirect evidence (similar to the previous maxim). Here, the employee's possession of unexplained wealth is treated as a kind of confession of misappropriation. The story of al-Harith ibn Wahb shows that 'Umar did not accept the excuse of trading with official funds; he considered it betrayal. 'Umar's saying "For every traitor I have two trustees: water and clay" means that even if the traitor hides his wealth, the building he constructs with water and clay (i.e., his conspicuous consumption) becomes evidence against him. The author then connects this to modern laws on "unlawful gains" (kasb ghair mashru') that require officials to declare their assets, and notes that such Islamic principles were the origin of these legal remedies. The maxim itself (al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrarihi) is used in Hanafi fiqh to enforce confessions in all cases, including criminal and civil matters.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A person's own admission or confession that establishes a right or obligation against himself.
مُؤَاخَذ (mu'akhadh): Held responsible; taken to account.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which principle did Caliph 'Umar apply when he found an employee with unexplained wealth?
A) The employee's silence is acceptance.
B) The employee is held by his confession (the maxim).
C) The wealth is considered a gift from the public.
D) The employee must be given a loan from the treasury.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrarihi' is a cornerstone of evidence law in Hanafi fiqh, binding a person by his own voluntary confession, with the crucial exception of coercion.

The Thirty-Eighth Qa'idah: Evidence Establishes Certainty Like Ocular Sight
Definition and explanation of the 38th qa'idah about judicial proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'idah 'what is proven by evidence is like what is proven by ocular sight' to judicial scenarios, distinguishing between evidence-based certainty and mere illusion.

Core Idea
Evidence-based certainty in court equals direct observation — judge rules on what is proven, not hidden realities.

The 38th qa'idah from al-Majalla is a cornerstone of Islamic judicial procedure. It establishes that when a judge accepts evidence (bayyinah) in court, that evidence creates a legal certainty equivalent to having witnessed the event with one's own eyes. This lesson unpacks the qa'idah's meaning, its scope, and its practical application in fatwa and judgment, showing why judges must not be swayed by speculative possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qa'idah and its source
State the full Arabic text and note it is the 38th qa'idah of al-Majalla (article 80). Recognize that 'burhan' here means judicial proofs (bayyinah).
The qa'idah is also referenced in other Hanafi usul works.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'thabit bil burhan'
Understand that what is established by evidence (bayyinah) before a judge is treated as a real event, as if it were seen. The legal effect is that the judge must rule accordingly.
This is a principle of judicial finality, not epistemological certainty.
3
Examine the role of possibilities (tawahhum)
The passage explicitly notes that mere possibilities (e.g., witnesses might be liars) are disregarded because they are illusions (mawhum). The judge is not to investigate hidden realities.
This ties to another qa'idah: 'لا عبرة للتوهم' (no regard for illusion).
4
Apply the qa'idah to a concrete scenario
Consider a case where a judge accepts testimony of two witnesses, but later doubts arise about their honesty. According to this qa'idah, the judgment stands because the evidence was apparent and established.
This does not apply if counter-evidence (bayyinah mu'aridah) is presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۸/۸۱ - القاعدة الثامنة والثلاثون: الثابت بالبرهان كالثابت بالعيان
.(vo/p)
به
المراد بالبرهان الأدلة القضائية المثبتة التي تسمى بالبينات.
أي أن ما ثبت لدى القاضي في مجلس القضاء بالبينة، من الحوادث أو نتائجها الشرعية يعتبر أمراً واقعاً كأنه محسوس مُشَاهَد بالعيان. فيقضي اعتماداً على هذا الثبوت وإن كان هناك احتمال خلافه بسبب من الأسباب ككون الشهود كذبة متسترين بالصلاح، أو كوجود سبب مبرىء في الواقع لم يطلع عليه أحد، ونحو ذلك من الاحتمالات. ذلك لأن كل هذه الاحتمالات تبقى في حيّز الموهومات بالنسبة للبينة الظاهرة، وقد تقدم أنه لا عبرة للتوهم )) ف (١٤/٨١ . وأن مهمة القضاء البناء على ما يظهر ويثبت لديه، وليس القاضي مكلفاً باكتناه الحقائق في الواقع فإن هذا ليس في طاقته .
98/81 - The thirty-eighth rule: What is proven by evidence is like what is proven by ocular sight. (Majalla article 80). (vo/p) The intended meaning of 'evidence' (burhan) is the affirmative judicial proofs that are called 'bayyinat' (evidences). That is, that which is established before the judge in the court of judgment by evidence, of incidents or their legal consequences, is considered a real matter as if it were felt and seen by the eye. So he judges based on this establishment even if there is a possibility of its contrary due to some reasons, such as the witnesses being liars disguised as righteous, or the existence of an exculpatory cause in reality that no one knew about, and similar possibilities. That is because all these possibilities remain in the realm of illusions relative to the apparent evidence. And it has already been stated that 'there is no regard for illusion' (f/14/81). And that the task of judgment is to build upon what appears and is established before him, and the judge is not charged with penetrating into the realities of the actual world, for this is beyond his capacity.
Source pages p. 989

Commentary
This lesson presents the thirty-eighth qa'idah from al-Majalla: 'What is proven by evidence is like what is proven by ocular sight.' The passage type is a qa'idah definition, as it defines the term 'burhan' (evidence) and explains the ruling. The author clarifies that 'burhan' here refers to the legal proofs called 'bayyinat' (evidences). The meaning is that when a judge, in his court, determines a fact or its legal consequences based on evidence (bayyinah), that fact is treated as real and certain, as if it were directly observed. Consequently, the judge passes judgment on that basis, even if there is a possibility that the reality is different—for example, if the witnesses are actually dishonest or if there is an exculpatory fact not known to the court. Why? Because such possibilities are mere illusions (mawhum) compared to the apparent evidence. The author references another qa'idah: 'there is no regard for illusion' (la 'ibrata lil-tawahhum). Thus, the judge's duty is to base his ruling on what appears and is established before him; he is not required to uncover absolute reality, as that is beyond human capacity. This qa'idah is fundamental in judicial procedure, ensuring that judgments are not suspended due to speculative doubts.

Key Terms
برهان (burhan): Evidence or proof that establishes a fact in judgment; often synonymous with bayyinah in this context
بينة (bayyinah): The evidence presented in court, typically witnesses or other proofs
موهوم (mawhum): An illusion or mere possibility that has no weight in legal judgment

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the thirty-eighth qa'idah, what is the judge's task regarding evidence?
A) To uncover absolute reality.
B) To base his ruling on what is established by evidence before him, ignoring mere possibilities.
C) To always disregard witness testimony if there is any doubt.
D) To summon witnesses until the truth is known.

Key Takeaway
The judge must base his ruling on the evidence established in court and disregard mere possibilities, as if the proven fact were directly witnessed.

The Maxim: Evidence for Contrary, Oath for Original
Stating the maxim that evidence is used to prove contrary to the apparent, while the oath preserves the original state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, explain its meaning in Hanafi procedure, and apply it to allocate burden of proof in a typical case.

Core Idea
Evidence overcomes defaults; oath preserves the status quo when proof fails.

This maxim is a cornerstone of Hanafi procedural law. It clarifies the respective roles of evidence and oath in litigation: evidence is brought to establish a fact that contradicts what is normally assumed, while the oath safeguards the original state when evidence is lacking. The maxim directly informs the classic rule 'البيّنة على المدّعي واليمين على من أنكر' (Evidence is upon the claimant, the oath upon the denier).

Guided Path
1
Recite the maxim in Arabic and English
Memorize the wording: 'البينة لإثبات خلاف الظاهر، واليمين لإبقاء الأصل' – 'Evidence is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the original state.'
This is a primary maxim (qāʿida kulliyya) of intermediate rank (wusṭā).
2
Define the key components: ẓāhir and aṣl
The 'apparent' (ẓāhir) is the default observable state; the 'original' (aṣl) is the legally presumed state (e.g., non-liability, ownership from possession). Understanding these is crucial.
The text says meanings will be detailed later; for now, grasp the conceptual distinction.
3
Explain the legal function of evidence and oath
Evidence (bayyina) is used to overturn the ẓāhir or aṣl – it proves a contrary reality. The oath (yamīn) is not to prove but to maintain the original/default position when evidence is insufficient.
The oath does not 'prove' but 'preserves' – a subtle but important difference.
4
Connect to the burden of proof rule
This maxim underlies the well-known rule: 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر' (Evidence is on the claimant, the oath on the denier). The claimant seeks to prove contrary to the apparent; the defendant swears to keep the original.
The derived rule is listed as related in the analytical toolbox.
5
Apply to a sample case
Consider a dispute over a loan: The lender claims a debt; the borrower admits the loan but claims repayment. The original state (aṣl) is that the debt exists. The borrower's claim of repayment is contrary to the apparent (ẓāhir of continuing liability). Therefore, the borrower must bring evidence. If he fails, the lender's oath preserves the original debt.
Note who is 'claimant' in the second claim – the borrower becomes claimant for the payment.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents a key procedural maxim in Hanafi law. The rule states that evidence is used to establish a situation that contradicts what is normally apparent, while the oath is used to maintain the original state of affairs. The author notes that the detailed meanings will be discussed later, and that the full understanding of this rule comes from the next derived rule (101/81). This maxim underlies the allocation of burden of proof: the claimant must bring evidence to overcome the presumption, and the defendant's oath preserves the default position.

Key Terms
ظاهر (ẓāhir): The apparent state of affairs; what is normally assumed.
أصل (aṣl): The original state; e.g., the presumption of innocence or non-liability.

B) To preserve the original state.
C) To challenge evidence.
D) To delay judgment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'Evidence for contrary, oath for original' allocates burden of proof: the claimant must prove deviation from the norm; the defendant's oath safeguards the default.

The Maxim of Contradiction: No Proof, But Ruling May Be Confused
Stating the maxim that contradictory claims invalidate proof but may disturb the judge's ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim of contradiction, explain its two rulings, and apply it to a witness testimony scenario.

Core Idea
Contradiction voids proof but only confuses the judge's decision.

This lesson introduces a key procedural maxim from the Majalla (Article 80) that governs the effect of contradictory statements in legal proceedings. It distinguishes between the complete invalidation of proof (ḥujja) and the mere disturbance (ikhtilāl) of the judge's ruling. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating evidence and judicial decisions.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Learn the Arabic text and its literal translation. Break it into two parts: (1) 'لا حجة مع التناقض' (no proof with contradiction) and (2) 'لكن يختل معه حكم الحاكم' (but the judge's ruling becomes confused with it).
Focus on the contrast between 'no proof' (absolute) and 'confused ruling' (relative).
2
Understand the Two Rulings
The first part declares that contradictory evidence or claims lose their probative value; they cannot serve as proof. The second part states that the judge's ruling based on such evidence becomes disturbed or undermined (yukhtallu), not automatically invalid.
The judge may still uphold the ruling if other evidence supports it; the contradiction does not automatically overturn it.
3
Apply to a Witness Testimony Case
Consider a witness who first states the contract was at noon, then later says it was at sunset. Under this maxim, his contradictory testimony is no longer valid proof; the judge, if he had already relied on it, must reconsider his ruling.
The witness's testimony is rejected, but if the judge had additional independent evidence, his ruling may remain intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
102/81 - القاعدة المتممة الأربعين: لا حجة مع التناقض، لكن يختل معه حكم الحاكم (م/ ٨٠).
102/81 - The fortieth completing rule: There is no proof with contradiction, but the judge's ruling becomes confused with it.
Source pages p. 991

Commentary
This is a concise maxim stating that when a person contradicts himself in his evidence or claims, his proof is invalid. However, the judge's ruling may be disturbed due to the contradiction. This rule serves to maintain consistency in legal arguments. It is a general principle in judicial procedure.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanāquḍ): Contradiction in statements or evidence that undermines credibility.
حجة (ḥujja): Proof or evidence that establishes a claim in court.
يختل (yukhtallu): Becomes confused, disturbed, or undermined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule, what happens to the judge's ruling when contradiction is present?
A) It is automatically overturned.
B) It becomes confused (yukhtallu).
C) It is strengthened.
D) It is ignored.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction in evidence destroys its probative value but does not automatically nullify the judge's ruling; it only casts doubt.

Refining the Qawida: He Who Hastens... – Broader Scope and Application
Critique and expansion of the classical qawida with new examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically evaluate the traditional formulation of the maxim 'He who hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation,' identify its narrow scope, and apply the author's broader formulation to new cases involving bad faith.

Core Idea
The maxim punishes bad faith, not just haste; even lawful means become invalid if the goal is prohibited.

This lesson examines a well-known Hanafi legal maxim as refined by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa. You will learn why the traditional version from the Majalla is too narrow and how the author proposes a broader formulation covering any action based on bad faith, regardless of the means. The lesson also presents a Maliki example to illustrate the principle in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional maxim and its limitation
State the classical wording: 'من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه.' Note that the Majalla restricts its application to cases where evil intent is directly tied to the hastiness.
The traditional formulation narrows the maxim by requiring both haste and bad faith.
2
Understand the author's critique
Recognize two flaws: (1) the scope is limited to evil intent connected to haste, while the reality is broader; (2) there are too few examples in the commentaries.
The author argues the maxim should cover all actions based on bad faith, even if the means are lawful.
3
Analyze the broader scope
The broader formulation includes any action (tasarrruf) founded on bad faith (sū' al-niyyah), aimed at a prohibited result, even if the means are outwardly lawful.
The key element is bad faith, not the hastiness itself.
4
Examine the Maliki example on divorce
Imam Malik ruled that if a man divorces his wife, takes her back during iddah, then divorces again without consummation intending to harm her, the iddah does not restart.
This illustrates the maxim: the husband hastened to harm by using lawful divorce, so he is punished by not restarting the iddah.
5
Apply the broader formulation
Use the principle to evaluate similar cases, e.g., intercepting trade caravans (talaqqī al-rukbān) with bad faith. Note that the other three schools (Hanafi, Shafi'i, Hanbali) did not follow Malik in the divorce ruling, showing ikhtilaf.
Understanding this maxim helps in analyzing modern ḥiyal (legal stratagems) where a lawful form hides an unlawful goal.

Source Text

Chapter Ten: The General Maxims in Fiqh. Connected to that are the maxims that contain the philosophy of the system of proof in Islamic law, and judicial preference between the conflicting claims of two opponents. Supplement to Chapter Eighty-One: A New Formulation with Evidence for the Maxim 'He Who Hastens a Thing...'
104/81 - We presented in the previous chapter the twelfth maxim: 'He who hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation' (F: 62/81) and explained it briefly.
Two observations are noted about this maxim: The first is that its traditional formulation as brought by the Majalla narrows the scope of the maxim, limiting it to evil intent connected with hastiness. While the reality of the maxim is much broader than that, as it includes all actions that are based on bad faith and intent even if the person uses a lawful means for it, as long as his goal is to reach a prohibited result.
The second observation on the maxim is the scarcity of examples mentioned under it in the commentaries of the Majalla. For these reasons, we will present a number of additional examples and evidence, then propose a better and more general formulation for this maxim.
The first example: Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) holds that if a man divorced his wife, then took her back before the end of her waiting period, then divorced her without having touched her, if he intended thereby to harm her by prolonging the waiting period, she does not begin a new waiting period, but rather builds upon what has passed of her waiting period (1).
The second example: Imam Malik holds that one who is prohibited from intercepting [trade caravans]... (1) Dr. Fathi al-Darini: Abuse of Right, doctoral dissertation, p. 262. It is noted that the other three schools did not agree with Malik in this ijtihad.
Source pages p. 993

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the well-known Hanafi legal maxim 'من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه' (He who hastens a thing before its time is punished by its deprivation). The author, Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, points out two shortcomings in the traditional formulation as codified in the Majalla. First, the traditional wording restricts the maxim to cases where there is evil intent directly tied to the haste, but the author argues the maxim's true scope is broader: it covers any action founded on bad faith (sū' al-niyyah) even if the means used are outwardly lawful, provided the ultimate goal is a prohibited result. Second, the commentaries on the Majalla provide too few illustrative examples. To remedy this, the author proposes a more comprehensive formulation and offers additional examples from the Maliki school (though the work itself is Hanafi, the examples serve to illustrate the principle). The first example involves a man who divorces his wife, reconciles during the iddah, then divorces her again without consummation. Imam Malik ruled that if the husband's intent was to harm her by prolonging the iddah, the second divorce does not restart the waiting period—she continues from where she left off. The second example (cut off) likely concerns a prohibition on intercepting trade caravans (talaqqī al-rukbān), where the interceptor's bad faith leads to legal consequences. The author notes that the other three schools (Hanafi, Shafi'i, Hanbali) did not follow Malik in this specific ruling, highlighting the diversity of ijtihad.

Key Terms
قاعدة كلية (qā‘idah kulliyyah): A general legal maxim that applies to multiple chapters of fiqh.
سوء النية (sū‘ al-niyyah): Bad faith or ill intent; the key element that triggers the maxim's application beyond mere haste.
استعجال (isti‘jāl): Hastiness or rushing to achieve something before its proper time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main problem with the traditional formulation of the maxim 'من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه' as found in the Majalla?
A) It is too broad and includes too many exceptions.
B) It limits the maxim to evil intent directly tied to hastiness, ignoring broader bad-faith actions.
C) It only applies to commercial transactions and not to family law.
D) It is an accurate restatement of Imam Malik's view.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'He who hastens...' is not limited to haste with evil intent; it applies to any action grounded in bad faith aimed at a prohibited result.

Refining the Maxim: From Haste to Bad Intention
Proposing a broader formulation for a legal maxim based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the proposed new formulation of the maxim on bad intention and apply it to cases where a person uses a legal stratagem to circumvent Sharia.

Core Idea
A legal act done with bad intention is treated contrary to the actor's purpose, protecting Sharia's objectives.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa critiques a classical legal maxim. The old maxim, 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it,' covers only cases of premature action. Al-Zarqa shows that many cases – such as debtors endowing property to evade creditors – involve bad faith, not haste. He proposes a broader maxim that focuses on the actor's ill intention, thereby protecting the maqasid of Sharia from manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original maxim
Recall the classical maxim: 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it.' Note its limited scope to temporal haste.
This maxim is well-known in Hanafi fiqh, e.g., early zakat payment.
2
Analyse the examples given
Examine the two examples: debtor endowing wealth to evade creditors, seller concealing a defect with a stipulation. In both, the actor's intention is bad, but there is no haste.
These share an element of deceit or bad faith.
3
Understand the deficiency
Recognise that the old maxim fails to cover these cases because they lack haste. The real common element is ill intention (sū' al-niyya).
The maxim's formulation is 'qasira' (narrow) and needs broadening.
4
Learn the proposed new wording
Memorise both proposed formulations: 'The ill-intentioned person is treated in the transaction contrary to his intention' or 'Whoever intends by his transaction an illegitimate purpose is treated contrary to his objective.'
They shift focus from haste to maqasid.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Practice applying the new maxim to cases like a husband divorcing his wife to deprive her of inheritance, or a company transferring assets to avoid debt repayment.
Always check the intention: is it to circumvent Sharia?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يعامل السيئ النية في التصرف بنقيض قصده»، أو «من قصد بتصرفه غرضاً غير مشروع عومل بنقيض مقصوده .
Appendix to Section (81): New formulation for the maxim 'Whoever hastens something...' ... which depends on the settlement of debt from his assets, absolutely, because of what some debtors resort to by endowing their assets to smuggle them from creditors. This, as you see, is also treating the ill-intentioned debtor contrary to his intention. This is in addition to other examples, such as the seller concealing a defect in the merchandise while knowing it, along with his stipulation on the buyer that he is free from every defect appearing in the merchandise. We have already stated that according to the majority of jurists (other than the Hanafis), the option of defect is established for the buyer, and the deceitful intention of the ill-intentioned seller is repelled against him. 109/81 - A proposed new formulation for this maxim: It is observed in the four preceding examples that in none of them is there a hastening of something before its legal time, as depicted by the mentioned maxim. Rather, these examples, along with what preceded them in paragraph 62 of this chapter, all share the presence of bad faith on the part of the acting person, and in punishing him by repelling his intention against him. Thus, jurisprudence necessitated treating him contrary to what he intended, to protect the legal rulings from being manipulated by the obligated person, turning them away from their objectives for which they were legislated, and thereby harming others. This is in reality the spirit of the maxim, the formulation of which fell short and is narrower than its comprehensive legislative goal. In light of this observation, the examples, and the evidence, we propose for this maxim one of the following two formulations: 'The ill-intentioned person is treated in the transaction contrary to his intention,' or 'Whoever intends by his transaction an illegitimate purpose is treated contrary to his objective.'
Source pages p. 996

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a well-known legal maxim. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, points out that the existing maxim 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it' is too narrow. He presents examples—debtors endowing wealth to evade creditors, sellers concealing defects—where the problem is not haste but bad faith. The Hanafi position is that the maxim should be reworded to focus on the ill intention (sū' al-niyya) rather than haste. The author proposes two new wordings: 'The ill-intentioned person is treated in the transaction contrary to his intention' and 'Whoever intends by his transaction an illegitimate purpose is treated contrary to his objective.' This reflects a broader legal philosophy: protecting the objectives of Sharia from manipulation.

Key Terms
سوء النية (sū' al-niyya): Bad faith or ill intention in a legal transaction, where the person acts with a purpose contrary to the objectives of Sharia.
قصد (qaṣd): Intention or purpose behind a legal act; the aim that the actor seeks to achieve.
استعجال (isti'jal): Hastening, performing an act before its legally prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proposed new formulation, how should a debtor who endows his property specifically to evade creditors be treated?
A) The endowment is valid and creditors cannot touch it.
B) The endowment is ineffective and the property remains subject to debt settlement.
C) The debtor is punished by imprisonment.
D) The endowment is valid but the debtor must pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
The maxim on bad intention is broader than the hastening maxim; it treats the ill-intentioned actor contrary to his purpose to protect Sharia's objectives from manipulation.

Conflict Between Presumption and Circumstantial Evidence
Establishes rule that circumstantial evidence prevails over presumption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'circumstantial evidence always prevails over the original presumption' to evidentiary disputes and determine which party's claim is accepted based on the essence of the dispute.

Core Idea
Circumstantial evidence overrides original presumption because it indicates a change and reveals the essence of the dispute.

In Hanafi evidentiary theory, not all presumptions are equal. When an original presumption (asl) is contradicted by clear circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal), the latter prevails. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this core maxim and demonstrates its application in three classical cases: the deposit dispute, the father-son maintenance case, and the mill water case. Understanding this maxim is essential for any mufti or judge tasked with weighing evidence.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core maxim
Begin by internalizing the rule: 'When the original presumption and circumstantial evidence conflict, the circumstantial evidence is always given preponderance.' Understand that circumstantial evidence is treated as an indication (qarina) that signals a change in the presumed state.
This maxim is considered an intermediate (wusta) qa'ida in Hanafi legal theory.
2
Analyze the first example: deposit (wadi'ah)
The depositary claims return; the depositor denies. Apparent conflict is about return, but the essence is liability. By looking at the essence (jawhar al-niza'), the original presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimmah) supports the depositary. This illustrates that not every asl is the same—the relevant asl is determined by the essence.
The maxim 'al-ibra li al-maqasid wa al-ma'ani' (weight is given to purposes and meanings) governs here.
3
Analyze the second example: father-son maintenance
If the father is wealthy at the time of litigation, this circumstantial evidence overcomes the original presumption of non-wealth, making the father liable for what he spent from his son's wealth. If the father is poor, the original presumption holds and the father is not liable. The present state of wealth is taken as evidence of the past.
This is a case of 'inverse istishab' (al-istishab al-maqlub).
4
Understand inverse istishab
Normal istishab extends a past state into the present. Inverse istishab reverses this: the present state (wealth at litigation) is extended backward to the past. This is allowed only when the present state provides a strong circumstantial indication.
The text emphasizes that this is called 'inverse' because it reverses the usual direction of istishab.
5
Apply to a new case
Using the maxim, decide a new dispute: Suppose a buyer claims he paid the price, the seller denies, and the buyer shows a bank transfer receipt from the transaction date. The receipt is circumstantial evidence. Apply the maxim to determine which party's claim is preponderant.
The receipt, though not a direct confession, is a qarina that supports the buyer.

مثال ذلك: مالو ادعى الوديع ردَّ الوديعة، وأنكر المالك المودع ردها إليه ولا بينة فالقول الراجح قول الوديع، لأنَّ جوهر هذا النزاع يرمي إلى تضمين الوديع قيمة الوديعة بزعم أنه جحدها أو إلى عدم تضمينه بزعم ردها، وإن كان ظاهر النزاع منصباً على رد وديعة أو عدمه.
فبالنظر إلى جوهر النزاع، وباعتبار أنَّ العبرة للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ، يشهد للوديع أصل براءة الذمة.
وبالنظر إلى الصورة الظاهرة يشهد للمودع أنّ الأصل عدم الرد لكونه عارضاً، والأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم.
فيرجح اعتبار أصل براءة الذمة هنا، لأنه هو الذي يتعلق به جوهر النزاع، ولأنه تؤيده أيضاً قاعدة كون الأمين مصدقاً في قوله، حتى يثبت تعديه أو تقصيره .
وهذا يفسر لنا أن كثيراً من الفروع المستثناة من بعض القواعد المتقدمة إنما استثنيت منها لأنها أجدر بالتفريع على قاعدة أخرى .. ب - وإن كان التعارض بين الأصل ودلالة الحال ترجح دلالة الحال دائماً لأنها قرائن وشواهد قائمة تنبىء بحدوث أمر يغير حالة الأصل. فيكون بمثابة دليل على كذب تمسك من يتمسك بذلك الأصل.
مثال ذلك: فرع اختلاف الأب والابن في يسار الأب وإعساره حين إنفاقه على نفسه من مال ابنه كما تقدم.
فإنه إذا كان الأب حين الخصومة موسِراً كان هذا أمارة ظاهرة على صحة دعوى الابن ويكون القول له في أنَّ أباه كان موسراً، فيضمن الأب ما أنفق على نفسه من مال ابنه أن الأصل عدم اليسار.
وإذا كان الأب حين الخصومة معسراً كان الأمر بالعكس، أي أنه يكون القول للأب عملاً بالأصل، لأن الغنى أمر عارض.
فهنا حينما شهدت دلالة الحال للابن (حال يسار الأب حين الخصومة) رجحت على الأصل الذي يتمسك به الأب وهو عدم الغنى. وكذا يقال في كل فروع تحكيم الحال التي تسمى: مسائل الاستصحاب المقلوب.

فيسَار الأب في حال الخصومة قد انسحب على الماضي لأنه اعتبر دليلاً على يساره حين الإنفاق.
ومثال ذلك يلحظ في قضية الاختلاف في انقطاع ماء الطاحون المتقدمة.
Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh. Example: If the depositary claims to have returned the deposit, and the owner-depositor denies the return and there is no evidence, the preponderant statement is that of the depositary, because the essence of this dispute aims at imposing liability on the depositary for the value of the deposit on the claim that he denied it, or not imposing liability on the claim of return, even if the apparent dispute is about the return of a deposit or not. So by looking at the essence of the dispute, and considering that weight is given to purposes and meanings not to words, the original presumption of innocence of liability supports the depositary. And by looking at the apparent form, the original presumption of non-return supports the depositor because it is an incidental matter, and the original presumption in incidental matters is non-existence. So the consideration of the original presumption of innocence of liability is preferred here, because it is what the essence of the dispute attaches to, and because it is also supported by the maxim that the trustee is believed in his statement until his transgression or negligence is proven. And this explains to us that many branches exempted from some of the preceding maxims are only exempted because they are more deserving to be subsumed under another maxim. b - And if there is conflict between the original presumption and circumstantial evidence, the circumstantial evidence is always given preponderance because they are existing indications and evidences that indicate the occurrence of a matter that changes the state of the original presumption. So it is like evidence of the falsehood of the adherence of the one who adheres to that original presumption. Example: The branch of dispute between father and son regarding the father's wealth and poverty at the time of his spending on himself from his son's wealth as previously mentioned. If the father at the time of litigation is wealthy, this is an apparent indication of the validity of the son's claim, and the son's statement is accepted that his father was wealthy, so the father is liable for what he spent on himself from his son's wealth, even though the original presumption is non-wealth. And if the father at the time of litigation is poor, the opposite is the case, i.e., the father's statement is accepted by operation of the original presumption, because wealth is an incidental matter. So here when circumstantial evidence supported the son (the case of the father's wealth at the time of litigation), it was given preponderance over the original presumption that the father adheres to, which is non-wealth. And so it is said in all branches of judging by circumstances, which are called: cases of inverse istishab. And it is called inverse, because normal istishab is considering the past existence extending to the present time (see: paragraph 7/81), while this involves considering the present existence as the basis for the past. So the father's wealth at the time of litigation has been extended to the past because it is considered evidence of his wealth at the time of spending. And an example of that is seen in the previously mentioned case of dispute regarding the cessation of the mill's water.
Source pages p. 1002

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of a core maxim in Hanafi evidence law: when an original presumption (asl) conflicts with circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal), the circumstantial evidence prevails. The author begins with the example of a depositary (al-wadi). The depositary claims he returned the deposit; the depositor denies. The apparent conflict is about the act of return, but the essence of the dispute is whether the depositary is liable. By focusing on the essence (جوهر النزاع) and applying the maxim 'al-ibra li al-maqasid wa al-ma'ani', the depositary is supported by the original presumption of innocence (براءة الذمة). Even though the depositor is supported by the presumption that incidental matters (like return) are absent until proven, the essential presumption is given priority. The author then states the general rule: in conflict between asl and dalalat al-hal, dalalat al-hal always prevails because it indicates a change. The example of the father and son illustrates this: if the father is wealthy at the time of litigation, this circumstantial evidence overcomes the original presumption of non-wealth, making the father liable. Conversely, if the father is poor, the original presumption of non-wealth leads to his exoneration. This is called 'istishhab maqlub' (inverse istishhab) because it applies the present state to the past. The passage underscores the Hanafi method of prioritizing substantive reality over formal presumptions, and demonstrates how many exceptions to other maxims are actually subsumed under this more fundamental maxim.

Key Terms
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Circumstantial evidence; existing indications and signs that suggest a fact, used to override a presumption
الاستصحاب المقلوب (al-istiṣḥāb al-maqlūb): Inverse istishab: applying the present state to the past, rather than the normal rule of extending the past to the present
جوهر النزاع (jawhar al-nizāʿ): The essence or real substance of the dispute, distinguished from its apparent form

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conflict between the original presumption (asl) and circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal), which is given precedence according to this passage?
A) The original presumption always
B) Circumstantial evidence always
C) It depends on the nature of the case
D) The original presumption except in property disputes

Key Takeaway
Circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal) always overrides the original presumption (asl) when they conflict, because it indicates a change and reveals the essence of the dispute.

Al-Qawaid al-Kubra — The Five Major Maxims

Muthbitat Override Presumptions: The Rule of Final Affirmers
Establishes that four affirmers override any presumption or circumstantial evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four muthbitat (legal affirmers), apply the rule that they override initial preponderators, and use the maxim 'al-bayyina li-ithbati khilaf al-zahir wa al-yamin li-ibqa' al-asl' in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
Muthbitat (bayyina, iqrar, nukul, yamin) override all presumptions and circumstantial evidence.

In Hanafi legal procedure, not all proofs are equal. Some are merely initial indicators—presumptions (usul) or circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal)—that guide us when no stronger proof exists. But when a muthbit (affirmer) appears, it decisively settles the matter. This lesson explains why these four affirmers—bayyina (formal evidence), iqrar (confession), nukul (refusal to swear), and yamin (oath)—are given ultimate authority, and how the classical maxim encapsulates their roles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four muthbitat
List the four affirmers mentioned: bayyina (formal evidence), iqrar (confession), nukul (refusal to take an oath), and yamin (oath). Note that the passage explicitly mentions the first three and the maxim includes the fourth (yamin).
The maxim highlights bayyina and yamin specifically, but the other two are also considered muthbitat.
2
Understand the conflict hierarchy
Recognise that muthbitat are 'final preponderators' (murajjihat niha'iyya). They overrule any 'initial preponderator' (murajjah ibtida'i)—i.e., a presumption (asl) or circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal)—because the muthbit is a clear proof (burhan sarih) that invalidates the opponent's reliance on mere appearance.
This is a qat'i hierarchy, not a probabilistic one.
3
Apply the hierarchy to the examples
Work through each example: creditor vs debtor. (1) Creditor brings bayyina → judgment for creditor (bayyina overcomes presumption of non-debt). (2) Debtor confesses → judgment against him (iqrar is decisive). (3) Creditor requests oath, debtor refuses → judgment against debtor (nukul treated as confession).
In each case, the muthbit leaves no room for the original presumption.
4
State and unpack the maxim
Read the maxim: 'al-bayyina li-ithbati khilaf al-zahir wa al-yamin li-ibqa' al-asl'. Explain each limb: bayyina proves what contradicts the apparent state (e.g., the apparent state is no debt; bayyina proves debt exists). The yamin (by the defendant) preserves the original presumption (e.g., if the defendant swears, the presumption of non-liability stands).
The maxim integrates both sides: evidence can overturn the default, while the oath protects it.
5
Connect to related qawaid
Relate this maxim to 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankar' (evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier). The two together form the core of Hanafi proof law.
Both are complementary; the first defines burden of proof, the second defines the function of each proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : أما إذا تعارض أحد المثبتات الأربعة المتقدمة مع أحد المرجحات الابتدائية فلا شك أنه يصار إلى الدليل المثبت، لأنه برهان صريح على عدم صحة ما يتمسك به الطرف الآخر من أصل أو دلالة حال .
وأمثلة ذلك مستفيضة كما لو ادعى دائن على مدين، فأنكر المدين الدين، فأقام المدعي بينة، أو أقرّ المدين، فإنه يحكم عليه. - وكذا لو طلب المدعي تحليفه اليمين فَنَكل المدعى عليه عن الحلف فإنه يحكم عليه.
وذلك لأن كلاً من الإقرار والنكول والبينة مرجّح نهائي يصار إليه لإدحاض دلالة الظاهر، وإثبات حدوث خلافه.
وعلى هذا الأساس وضعت القاعدة الفقهية القائلة: «البينة لإثبات خلاف الظاهر واليمين لإبقاء الأصل) (ف: ١٠٠/٨١).
Secondly: If one of the four previously mentioned affirmers contradicts one of the initial preponderators, there is no doubt that the affirmative evidence is resorted to, because it is clear proof of the invalidity of what the other party adheres to from a presumption or circumstantial evidence. Examples of that are abundant, such as if a creditor claims against a debtor, and the debtor denies the debt, and the claimant brings evidence (bayyina), or the debtor confesses (iqrar), then judgment is given against him. Likewise, if the claimant requests that he take an oath, and the defendant refuses (nukul) to swear, then judgment is given against him. That is because each of confession (iqrar), refusal (nukul), and evidence (bayyina) is a final preponderator that is resorted to in order to rebut the apparent indication (dalalat al-zahir) and to prove the occurrence of its contrary. And upon this basis, the fiqh maxim was formulated: 'Evidence (bayyina) is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath (yamin) is to preserve the original presumption.' (paragraph 100/81).
Source pages p. 1003

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on resolving conflicts in evidence by introducing the category of 'muthbitat' (affirmers). These are four: bayyina (formal evidence, typically two witnesses), iqrar (confession), nukul (refusal to take an oath after the opponent's request), and yamin (oath). The author states that when any of these affirmers conflicts with an initial preponderator (i.e., an original presumption or circumstantial evidence), the affirmer prevails without hesitation. This is because the affirmer is a clear proof (burhan sarih) that falsifies the opponent's reliance on mere presumptions. Examples are given: a creditor's claim supported by evidence, or the debtor's confession, or his refusal to take an oath—all result in judgment against him. The author then cites the well-known maxim: 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the yamin is to preserve the original presumption.' This maxim encapsulates the hierarchy: evidence can overcome the presumption of innocence or continuity, while the oath (by the defendant) is used to uphold the original presumption when the claimant cannot bring evidence. This passage is fundamental in Hanafi usul al-iftaa for understanding the strength of different types of proof in judicial proceedings.

Key Terms
المثبتات (al-muthbitāt): The four legal affirmers: bayyina (evidence), iqrar (confession), nukul (refusal to swear), and yamin (oath); they serve as final proofs
مرجح ابتدائي (murajjaḥ ibtidā'ī): Initial preponderators: the original presumption (asl) and circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal) that are used when there is no muthbit
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take a requested oath; in Hanafi law, it is treated as a confession and is a muthbit

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To prove a claim
B) To preserve the original presumption
C) To contradict the evidence
D) To establish circumstantial evidence

Key Takeaway
Muthbitat (evidence, confession, refusal to swear, and oath) override all initial presumptions and circumstantial evidence; evidence proves contrary to the apparent, while the oath preserves the original presumption.

The Maxim: Bayyina to Prove Contrary to the Apparent
Explains a procedural maxim and its furuat on testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-bayyina li-ithbat khilaf al-dhahir' to distinguish between admissible and inadmissible testimony on negation, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on witness numbers for limited negation.

Core Idea
Evidence overturns weak appearances; testimony on pure negation is rejected because it matches the original presumption.

This maxim is a cornerstone of Hanafi procedural law. It explains why the burden of proof falls on the claimant, and why testimony on pure negation is generally inadmissible. However, an exception exists for limited negation (e.g., alibi), opening an intra-Hanafi debate on witness numbers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Key Term
The maxim states 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent.' Recognize that 'apparent' here means weak apparent (al-dhahir al-da'eef) from a presumption or circumstance.
2
Link to the Rule on Burden of Proof
The maxim is the basis for 'Bayyina is upon the claimant, and the oath upon the denier.' Understand why: the claimant seeks to prove a right contrary to the original presumption of non-existence.
This is a crucial connection between procedural and substantive law.
3
Apply to Pure Negation
Testimony on pure negation (nafi mahd) is rejected because negation aligns with the original presumption, and witnesses cannot comprehensively confirm non-occurrence.
Examples: 'He did not return the deposit,' 'This is not his property.'
4
Apply to Limited Negation
Testimony on negation limited to a specific time or place is accepted, e.g., an alibi that the defendant was not at a particular location at a given time.
This is an exception due to the possibility of verification.
5
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Witness Numbers
Some Hanafi fuqaha require tawatur (widespread transmission) for limited negation, while usul scholars accept the ordinary witness quota. The author prefers the latter as more appropriate.
The Majallah adopted the tawatur view, but the stronger opinion in later Hanafi works is the ordinary quota.

كلمة الظاهر في قاعدة البينة لإثبات خلاف الظاهر» هو

ومعنى كون البينة تقام لإثبات خلاف الظاهر أنها تقام لإثبات خلاف
ما تدل عليه المرجحات الابتدائية .
وهذه القاعدة علة ومستند للقاعدة القائلة : البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر .
وذلك لأن المدعي يدعي ثبوت حق له على المدعى عليه. وهذا الحق أمرٌ ،عارض، فالأصل عدمه ولا يثبت إلا بمثبت. فيكون القول للمدَّعَى عليه الذي يتمسك بالظاهر، ولكن يحتفظ للمدعي بحق إثبات نشوء ذلك الحق الذي يدعيه على خصمه.

لا يشترطون ذلك. بل يكتفون بنصاب الشهادة العادي، وهذا أوجه.
لا
١٠٧٠
Chapter Ten: The Universal Legal Maxims. 8/82 - The meaning of 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent': The intended meaning of the word 'apparent' (dhahir) in the maxim 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent' is the weak apparent that is advanced, from a presumption (asl) or a circumstance (dalaalat hal). And the meaning of 'Bayyina is established to prove contrary to the apparent' is that it is established to prove contrary to what the preliminary preferrers (murajjihat ibtida'iyya) indicate. This maxim is the cause and basis for the maxim 'Evidence is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier.' That is because the claimant claims the establishment of a right for himself against the defendant. This right is an incidental matter, so the original presumption is its non-existence, and it is not established except by a proof (muthbit). Thus the statement (i.e., the benefit of the doubt) is for the defendant who clings to the apparent, but the claimant retains the right to prove the emergence of that right which he claims against his opponent. On this basis, the jurists decided that testimony is not accepted for pure negation (nafi mahd). So, for example, testimony is not accepted that the depositary did not return the deposit, or that this thing does not belong to so-and-so, or that so-and-so is not indebted to so-and-so, or that he did not do such-and-such. This is because negation is the original apparent, according to the principle that the original in incidental matters is non-existence, and evidence (bayyinat) is only needed to prove its opposite; and because testimony on negation is a rash act with obvious suspicion, for witnesses cannot encompass all times night and day, and the matter testified as non-existent may have occurred at a time they were unaware of. But if the negation is limited to a specific time, testimony on it is accepted, as if a person claims that another lent him an amount at a specific time and place, and the defendant brings trustworthy witnesses that he was not in that place at that time (see: Majallah article 1699 and its commentaries). (1) The jurists (fuqaha) in Hanafi fiqh branches stipulate that the number of witnesses for limited negation must be large enough to reach the level of tawatur (widespread transmission), and thus it was stated in the Majallah, but the usul scholars among them do not stipulate that; rather they are satisfied with the ordinary quota for testimony, and this is more appropriate. 1070
Source pages p. 1004

Commentary
This passage explains the maxim 'Bayyina is to prove contrary to the apparent.' The 'apparent' here refers to the weak apparent derived from a presumption or circumstantial indication. The maxim means that bayyina (evidence) is used to prove the opposite of what the preliminary preferrers indicate. This maxim also serves as the basis for the well-known rule 'Bayyina is upon the claimant, and the oath upon the denier.' The reasoning is that the claimant seeks to prove a right which is incidental; the original state is its non-existence, so the defendant's assertion based on the apparent is given weight, but the claimant may still prove the right. From this, the Hanafi jurists derived that testimony on pure negation is not accepted, because negation matches the original state, and witnesses cannot comprehensively confirm non-occurrence. However, an exception is made when the negation is limited to a specific time and place, such as an alibi. The footnote adds a Hanafi intra-school difference: some scholars require the number of witnesses for limited negation to reach the level of tawatur (as in the Majallah), while the usul scholars and the author hold that the ordinary witness quota suffices, which is considered the stronger view.

Key Terms
الظاهر الضعيف (al-dhahir al-da'eef): The weak apparent meaning that arises from a presumption (asl) or circumstance (dalaalat hal) which can be overturned by evidence.
النفي المحض (al-nafi al-mahd): Pure or absolute negation without restriction of time or place.
النفي المحصور (al-nafi al-mahsur): Negation limited to a specific time or place.
التواتر (al-tawatur): Widespread transmission that yields certainty; a large number of witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim discussed in this lesson, which type of testimony on negation is generally admissible?
A) Testimony that the depositary did not return the deposit at any time
B) Testimony that the defendant was not in the city on a specific date
C) Testimony that the claimant never owned the property
D) Testimony that a person is not indebted to another

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-bayyina li-ithbat khilaf al-dhahir' means evidence is used to overturn weak apparent presumptions, not to confirm the original state; thus testimony on pure negation is rejected, but limited negation is accepted, with the preferred Hanafi view accepting ordinary witness numbers.

The Maxim: Evidence Establishes Contrary to the Apparent – Meaning and Scope
Defining the maxim and its immediate consequences on proof rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'al-zahir' in the Hanafi evidentiary maxim and state the general rule that testimony is not accepted for pure negation, with its exception for limited negation.

Core Idea
Evidence overturns weak appearances; the default is non-existence, so pure negation needs no proof.

This lesson unpacks the foundational maxim of Hanafi evidence law: 'al-bayyina li ithbat khilaf al-zahir'. We will clarify what 'al-zahir' means in this context—it is not the obvious truth but a weak apparent derived from an origin or circumstance. This maxim underlies the allocation of burden of proof and explains why pure negation cannot normally be proved by testimony. We will also see the important exception when negation is limited to a specific time and place.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'al-zahir'
Distinguish between a strong apparent (e.g., the fact of possession indicating ownership) and the weak apparent referred to in this maxim—a default presumption from an origin or circumstance that can be overturned by evidence.
The weak apparent is what the maxim targets.
2
Understand why this maxim is the basis for 'al-bayyina ala al-mudda'i'
The claimant asserts an incidental right; the default is non-existence. The defendant clings to the apparent. Thus the burden of proof is on the claimant to provide evidence, while the defendant only needs an oath.
This is a direct consequence of the maxim.
3
Apply the rule to pure negation
Since negation is the apparent origin, testimony is not accepted for pure negation (e.g., 'the trustee did not return the trust'). Witnesses cannot comprehensively negate all times and places.
List examples from the text.
4
Recognize the exception: limited negation
When negation is confined to a specific time and place, witnesses can have knowledge, so testimony is accepted. Example: 'he was not at place X at time Y'.
Note the Hanafi ikhtilaf on number of witnesses (tawatur vs. normal count).
